Title: U.S.-Iran Tensions Escalate: Energy Markets Face Crisis
[Login](https://discoveryalert.com.au/login/)   [Free Alerts](#)

# U.S.-Iran Tensions Escalate: Global Energy Markets Brace for Crisis

BY [MUFLIH HIDAYAT](https://discoveryalert.com.au/author/muflih-hidayat/) ON APRIL 23, 2026

## Energy Markets Face Unprecedented Volatility as Regional Powers Contest Global Supply Routes

The transformation of energy security from a market-driven consideration into a geopolitical weapon marks perhaps the most significant shift in international relations since the end of the Cold War. As traditional suppliers leverage their positions to extract political concessions, consuming nations scramble to develop alternative partnerships that prioritise reliability over cost efficiency. This evolution extends beyond temporary price fluctuations, fundamentally restructuring how nations approach energy independence and strategic planning.

Maritime chokepoints have emerged as critical leverage points where regional conflicts translate into global economic consequences. The concentration of energy flows through narrow passages creates vulnerabilities that ambitious powers exploit to project influence far beyond their immediate territories. Understanding these dynamics becomes essential for predicting how energy markets will evolve as geopolitical tensions intensify across multiple regions simultaneously.

### When big ASX news breaks, our subscribers know first

## Regional Tensions Transform Global Energy Architecture

Current market indicators reveal the immediate impact of escalating **U.S.-Iran tensions escalate** across global energy systems. **WTI Crude trades at $93.27 (+0.33%)** while **Brent Crude reaches $102.70 (+0.79%)**, demonstrating how regional conflicts immediately affect worldwide pricing mechanisms. Furthermore, the divergent movement in **Natural Gas at $2.603 (-4.37%)** suggests traders differentiate between supply disruption risks and demand destruction concerns.

Critical chokepoint statistics underscore the strategic importance of current confrontations. Maritime traffic through the **Strait of Hormuz and Bab el-Mandeb Strait accounts for approximately 45% of global oil flows**, with the Hormuz passage alone handling **20% of worldwide oil transit routes**. Recent [reports from CNN](https://www.cnn.com/2026/04/23/world/live-news/iran-war-trump-blockade-israel-lebanon) indicate escalating tensions, with reports of Iran firing on passing ships causing traffic in the Strait of Hormuz to grind to a halt repeatedly.

The petroleum product complex shows mixed signals reflecting market uncertainty about supply security. **Heating Oil trades at $3.936 (-0.04%)** while **Gasoline reaches $3.373 (+0.43%)**, indicating selective concerns about specific product availability. Additionally, crude benchmarks reveal broader market stress, with **Murban Crude at $104.8 (+1.58%)** and the **OPEC Basket at $97.74 (-1.87%)** over recent trading sessions.

### Strategic Maritime Control Points

The concentration of global energy flows through narrow maritime passages creates immediate vulnerabilities when regional powers contest control. Current enforcement mechanisms extend beyond traditional sanctions, incorporating comprehensive maritime interdiction strategies that target energy export capabilities directly. These operations represent new models of economic pressure that could be replicated in future conflicts involving energy-producing nations.

Naval positioning around critical shipping lanes demonstrates how quickly regional tensions translate into global supply chain disruptions. Consequently, the strategic significance of these chokepoints means that even threats of closure or inspection regimes can trigger immediate market responses, as evidenced by current price volatility across energy commodities.

## Accelerated Development of Alternative Energy Alliances

American liquefied natural gas production has positioned Washington as the dominant global supplier, fundamentally altering international energy dependencies. **Current U.S. LNG export capacity processes approximately 18 billion cubic feet per day (Bcf/d)**, representing a dramatic expansion from **11.4 Bcf/d reached by the end of 2022**. The Energy Information Administration projects this **capacity will double by 2031** compared with 2024 levels, demonstrating sustained strategic investment in energy dominance.

Qatar's significant supply disruptions have accelerated shifts toward American suppliers. Following Iranian missile attacks in March on **Qatar's North Dome gas facilities**, Qatari officials estimate **repair time requiring three to five years** with Qatar extending force majeure on gas supplies potentially beyond mid-June 2026. This $20 billion LNG blackout forces major importers like Pakistan back to spot markets, with many considering switches to U.S. LNG supplies.

**Key Infrastructure Developments:**

* Iraq's first floating LNG (FLNG) import platform targets **500 million standard cubic feet per day (MMscf/d) baseline capacity**
* Technical ceiling capabilities reach **up to 1 Bcf/d** for expanded operations
* **Scheduled operational date: June 2026** under U.S. firm Excelerate operations
* KBR serves as consultant representing strategic U.S. technology partnership
* Five-year renewable agreement with Iraq's Electricity Ministry establishing long-term relationship

### Strategic Positioning Through Infrastructure Investment

The Iraq FLNG platform represents more than commercial partnership, serving as a strategic mechanism to reduce Iranian dependence while re-establishing American influence in Baghdad. According to analysis from energy industry sources, this facility represents both commercial partnership and strategic enhancement of Iraq's long-term energy security through reduced reliance on Iranian supplies.

European responses demonstrate coordinated diversification strategies following the 2022 Ukraine crisis. Major European oil and gas companies have moved aggressively to establish new ventures in Egypt, Libya, and Iraq, reducing traditional supply concentration. These developments represent permanent structural changes rather than temporary crisis responses, reflecting growing [energy transition challenges](https://discoveryalert.com.au/canada-economic-challenges-energy-transition-2025/) across the continent.

## Market Positioning Reflects Geopolitical Risk Assessment

Energy trading activity demonstrates sophisticated market positioning around geopolitical events. **Traders placed $430 million in positions minutes before Trump extended Iran ceasefire** negotiations, indicating active speculation around scenario outcomes. This trading volume suggests energy markets increasingly function as geopolitical risk assessment mechanisms rather than purely commodity exchanges.

Operational impacts extend across multiple sectors beyond direct energy trading. Air Canada scrapped key U.S. routes amid high fuel prices, while Lufthansa cancelled unprofitable European summer routes to save jet fuel consumption. These operational adjustments demonstrate how energy price volatility cascades through interconnected economic sectors.

Current supply indicators reveal structural tightening across global markets:

* **U.S. oil exports set to hit record highs** as domestic production exceeds consumption
* **Global oil inventories drift toward record lows** despite ongoing geopolitical tensions
* **Pakistan paying record premiums for fuel imports** following Qatar supply disruptions
* **Norway pumps near capacity as spare output buffer disappears** from global supply cushion

### Trading Pattern Analysis

The world's biggest physical oil trader warns of **months of price volatility** ahead, suggesting current market conditions represent structural rather than temporary disruptions. Oil markets no longer trade like traditional commodity markets, incorporating geopolitical risk premiums that persist regardless of immediate diplomatic outcomes.

Energy prices demonstrate divergent risk assessment across different commodities. While crude oil prices incorporate supply disruption premiums, [natural gas forecasts](https://discoveryalert.com.au/us-natural-gas-prices-forecast-2025/) reflect concerns about demand destruction from economic slowdown. This divergence suggests sophisticated market evaluation of different supply vulnerability scenarios.

## Scenario Planning for Extended Crisis Periods

**Diplomatic Resolution Scenario (Moderate Probability):**

Market impacts include oil prices potentially retreating to **$85-90/barrel range** with temporary reduction in geopolitical risk premiums. However, structural changes in energy relationships would persist even following diplomatic settlements. Normal shipping patterns could restore, but alternative supply arrangements developed during crisis periods remain operational.

**Extended Standoff Scenario (High Probability):**

Sustained price volatility with **$100+ oil becoming normalised** represents the most likely medium-term outcome. This scenario accelerates development of alternative supply routes and partnerships, potentially creating parallel energy systems aligned with different geopolitical blocs. Furthermore, permanent reconfiguration of global energy flows occurs regardless of eventual diplomatic resolution.

**Military Escalation Scenario (Low Probability, High Impact):**

Direct military confrontation could trigger **oil price spikes to $150-200/barrel** with fundamental disruption of global energy architecture. Emergency response mechanisms include strategic petroleum reserve releases and emergency LNG diversions to critical allies. This scenario requires pre-positioned crisis management protocols for supply disruption management.

### Strategic Reserve Management

China's strategic positioning demonstrates preparation for extended volatility periods. Beijing had built up LNG reserves during the year prior to the 2022 Ukraine conflict, providing insulation from immediate supply disruptions. This approach suggests anticipation of energy weaponisation in geopolitical competition.

Emergency allocation protocols become critical for managing crisis scenarios. Enhanced sanctions enforcement capabilities extend beyond traditional market mechanisms, incorporating comprehensive maritime interdiction strategies and alternative transportation route development.

## Long-Term Implications for Energy Security Framework

The emergence of distinct energy partnerships aligned with broader geopolitical alliances represents a fundamental departure from globalised energy markets. Current tensions accelerate formation of separate energy systems serving different political blocs, potentially creating permanent fragmentation in global energy trade.

**Investment Priorities Shifting Toward:**

* **Floating LNG terminals for enhanced supply flexibility** and reduced chokepoint dependence
* **Enhanced strategic reserve capacities** for extended crisis period management
* **Alternative transportation routes avoiding traditional chokepoints** through pipeline and shipping diversification
* **Advanced early warning systems for supply disruption management** incorporating geopolitical risk assessment

Technology developments focus on infrastructure resilience rather than efficiency optimisation. Multiple supplier arrangements replace single-source dependencies, whilst enhanced storage and buffer capacity provide cushions against supply disruptions. These infrastructure adaptations represent permanent changes in energy system architecture.

### Regulatory Framework Evolution

Governments develop new legal frameworks extending beyond traditional market mechanisms to include emergency allocation protocols and strategic partnership agreements. Enhanced sanctions enforcement capabilities incorporate maritime interdiction authorities and alternative route development mandates.

Policy coordination between allied nations becomes essential for effective crisis management. In addition, synchronised strategic reserve management and emergency supply sharing agreements require unprecedented international cooperation on energy security matters. These coordination mechanisms represent institutional changes that persist beyond immediate crisis periods.

### The next major ASX story will hit our subscribers first

## Risk Management Strategies for Market Participants

Energy companies and traders must develop sophisticated hedging strategies accounting for geopolitical risk factors as traditional price forecasting models prove inadequate for politically-driven market disruptions. Current trading patterns suggest fundamental changes in market structure requiring new analytical approaches.

**Infrastructure Resilience Requirements:**

* **Multiple supply source development** reducing single-supplier dependencies
* **Enhanced storage and buffer capacity** providing cushions against supply interruptions
* **Flexible transportation and processing infrastructure** enabling rapid supply source transitions
* **Advanced monitoring and early warning systems** incorporating geopolitical intelligence into operational planning

Crisis management protocols require pre-positioned response capabilities rather than reactive crisis response. Supply disruption scenarios must incorporate geopolitical escalation pathways, not just technical or commercial factors. Moreover, risk assessment frameworks expand beyond traditional commodity analysis to include political stability evaluation.

### Market Structure Adaptation

Energy markets increasingly function as geopolitical risk assessment mechanisms rather than pure commodity exchanges. Trading strategies must account for policy announcement timing, diplomatic negotiation outcomes, and military positioning intelligence alongside traditional supply-demand fundamentals.

Volatility persistence suggests structural market changes rather than temporary crisis impacts. Consequently, market participants require enhanced risk management capabilities incorporating scenario planning across multiple geopolitical outcomes. Traditional hedging instruments may prove insufficient for managing politically-driven price movements.

## What Does This Mean for Oil Production Strategies?

Current tensions simultaneously accelerate strategic considerations around [OPEC production impact](https://discoveryalert.com.au/opec-meeting-2025-oil-production-impact/) and complicate renewable energy transition efforts across multiple regions. Energy security concerns drive increased investment in domestic clean energy sources whilst immediate supply security needs reinforce reliance on traditional hydrocarbon infrastructure. This dual pressure creates complex transition pathway challenges.

**Key Transition Framework Adjustments:**

* **Accelerated domestic renewable energy development** prioritising energy independence over efficiency optimisation
* **Enhanced focus on energy independence versus cost efficiency** in policy framework development
* **Potential delays in phase-out timelines for strategic fossil fuel infrastructure** maintaining supply security capabilities
* **Increased investment in energy storage and grid resilience technologies** supporting supply security during transition periods

European responses demonstrate accelerated clean energy investment alongside enhanced fossil fuel diversification. Rooftop solar orders triple as gas prices surge, whilst clean energy funding targets $20 billion for developing economy projects. These parallel developments suggest energy security drives both renewable acceleration and fossil fuel supply diversification simultaneously.

### Strategic Infrastructure Balance

The resolution of current tensions will significantly influence whether global energy markets evolve toward greater integration or further fragmentation along geopolitical lines. Market participants must prepare for multiple scenarios whilst governments balance immediate security needs with long-term sustainability objectives.

Investment strategies require scenario planning across different geopolitical outcome pathways. Energy transition timelines may accelerate in some regions whilst extending in others, depending on supply security considerations and geopolitical alliance structures. For instance, this variability creates complex planning challenges for long-term energy infrastructure development.

Energy independence considerations increasingly influence renewable energy project location and timing decisions. Domestic energy production capabilities become strategic assets regardless of comparative cost advantages from international suppliers. This shift fundamentally alters renewable energy investment criteria beyond traditional economic factors.

## How Are Global Markets Adapting to New Tensions?

The current environment has highlighted significant vulnerabilities in global energy systems, particularly regarding [WTI & Brent dynamics](https://discoveryalert.com.au/oil-futures-wti-brent-global-energy-2025/) and regional price differentials. However, market adaptations are occurring rapidly across multiple sectors, with significant implications for long-term energy security.

The [oil price trade war](https://discoveryalert.com.au/oil-price-movements-trade-war-2025/) dynamics demonstrate how geopolitical tensions reshape traditional market relationships. According to analysis from the [Council on Foreign Relations](https://www.cfr.org/global-conflict-tracker/conflict/confrontation-between-united-states-and-iran), these structural changes extend beyond temporary crisis responses to fundamental alterations in global energy architecture.

As **U.S.-Iran tensions escalate** further, market participants must navigate increasingly complex scenarios involving supply disruption risks, alternative partnership development, and enhanced security requirements. These developments require sophisticated risk management approaches that incorporate geopolitical intelligence alongside traditional market analysis.

The resolution of current tensions will determine whether global energy markets evolve toward greater integration or further fragmentation along geopolitical lines. Therefore, strategic planning must account for multiple scenarios whilst maintaining operational flexibility to respond to rapidly changing geopolitical conditions.

**Disclaimer:** *This analysis is based on current market conditions and geopolitical developments as of the publication date. Energy markets involve significant volatility and risk factors beyond those discussed. Readers should conduct independent research and consult qualified advisors before making investment decisions. The scenarios presented represent analytical frameworks rather than predictions of specific outcomes.*

# Marketer-in-chief: Geopolitical shocks roil energy markets at the start of 2026

January 13, 2026

* * *

[Geopolitical tensions in Venezuela](https://www.energyaspects.com/resources/insights/venezuelas-oil-recovery-a-long-and-daunting-road)and Iran are firmly in the spotlight, setting the early-year tone for risk across[global energy markets.](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis)

For crude, any rebound in[Venezuelan](https://www.energyaspects.com/resources/insights/webinar-on-demand-venezuela-oil-supply-and-the-donroe-doctrine)output is expected to be gradual and incremental, but a shift in trade flows is already underway. In clean product markets, gasoline prices in the Atlantic basin have rallied at the start of the year, though softer fundamentals could soon put a lid on further gains. Meanwhile, diesel markets remain under pressure amid ample supply. Refining margins are hovering just above run-cut thresholds, and any reallocation of Venezuelan barrels is poised to have a far greater impact on[crude markets](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis/crude-oil)than on[refined products.](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis/oil-products)

[Geopolitical](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/multi-market-analysis/geopolitics)risks will remain elevated as the Iranian regime’s political crisis deepens, with intensifying protests and repeated warnings that the US could intervene. Oil and gas markets are bracing for a range of potential scenarios, each carrying sharply different implications.[Macro risks](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/multi-market-analysis/global-macro)are also front and centre, with mixed US data, shifting inflation and tariff uncertainty clouding the outlook. In gas markets, cold weather has tightened European balances and buoyed TTF, while JKM–TTF spreads have narrowed as freight rates collapse. In North America, mild weather and high storage continue to weigh on Henry Hub.

## Crude Oil

[Oil](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis)prices started 2026 on a volatile note, rebounding after initial losses tied to[Venezuela’s political turmoil.](https://www.energyaspects.com/resources/insights/webinar-on-demand-venezuela-oil-supply-and-the-donroe-doctrine)While traders weigh short-term supply risks, positioning tailwinds—including the unwinding of decade-high managed money shorts—are supporting prices.[Venezuelan output recovery will be slow,](https://www.energyaspects.com/resources/insights/venezuelas-oil-recovery-a-long-and-daunting-road)with legal and operational hurdles limiting gains. Immediate trade-flow shifts will come from Washington’s plans to market Venezuelan barrels, not increased production.

Despite ubiquitous bearish sentiment in the market, supply–demand balances may prove tighter than expected overall, as seen throughout 2025.

Figure 1: Venezuela crude exports, mb/d - Source: OilX, Energy Aspects

[![Image 8: Venezuela crude exports, mb/d ](https://lirp.cdn-website.com/572d1c0b/dms3rep/multi/opt/1768314816589-1920w.png)](https://www.energyaspects.com/)

## Oil Products

[Gasoline](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis)prices in the Atlantic basin have found support from refinery outages and increased CTA buying, but further gains are likely to be capped by rising inventories and subdued US demand. Meanwhile, diesel cracks remain under pressure, with the market well supplied thanks to strong runs in Europe and the US Gulf Coast, alongside steady inflows from the Middle East. Even with the EU’s “refining loophole” ban set to take effect on 21 January, we expect diesel margins to stay soft given the abundance of supply.

Figure 2: European diesel inventories, mb - Source: OilX, Energy Aspects

![Image 9: European diesel inventories, mb -
](https://lirp.cdn-website.com/572d1c0b/dms3rep/multi/opt/1768314983761-1920w.png)

## Refining

[Refining margins](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/oil-market-analysis/refining)are just above run-cut levels, supported by high utilisation rates. The potential reshuffle of Venezuelan crude flows is expected to have only a minor impact on product yields, with USGC refiners able to absorb additional heavy barrels. We forecast margins to average $5/bbl in 2026, slightly down y/y, but up from 2024, with stronger performance in H1 26.

## Europe Gas

Cold weather has tightened[European](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/gas-power-market-analysis/europe-gas)gas balances, supporting TTF prices. Our price-sensitivity model suggests Europe would have only 25 bcm in stocks by end-March at current market prices—down by 12 bcm y/y and 37 bcm below the 2023–24 average. This lack of late-winter stocks underpins our bullish view on TTF near-curve prices through the rest of winter and into 2026. The risk of a Russia–Ukraine peace deal remains a key downside factor for prices.

Figure 3: European storage level, bcm - Source: Energy Aspects, GIE

![Image 10: European storage level, bcm ](https://lirp.cdn-website.com/572d1c0b/dms3rep/multi/opt/1768315133500-1920w.png)

## North America Gas

Mild weather and high storage levels continue to weigh on Henry Hub prices, despite strong LNG feedgas demand and record production. The market remains loose, with the next tightening event likely to come from the ramp-up of feedgas flows to[Golden Pass.](https://www.energyaspects.com/resources/insights/golden-pass-still-on-track-for-first-lng-exports-this-march-but-risk-of-slippage)

## Global LNG

JKM–TTF spreads have narrowed as freight rates collapse and supply to east-of-Suez markets increases. A significant y/y boost to baseload east-of-Suez supply is expected this year from the full ramp-up of[LNG](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/gas-power-market-analysis/global-lng)Canada, reducing the need for West African cargoes and shifting more US spot cargoes towards Asia.

## Geopolitics

The[geopolitical](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/multi-market-analysis/geopolitics)premium in oil prices is set to persist as focus shifts from Venezuela to Iran. Widespread protests continue despite a mounting death toll and intensified government crackdown. Markets are weighing scenarios from supply disruptions to regime change. President Trump reiterated possible US intervention, though officials are likely to recommend economic and cyber measures over military action. Israel is also considering ways to pressure Iran.

## Marco

[Macro](https://www.energyaspects.com/energy-markets/multi-market-analysis/global-macro)uncertainty persists, with mixed US economic data, shifting inflation and potential tariff changes keeping risks elevated. The outlook remains clouded, with markets sensitive to new developments on both the economic and geopolitical fronts.

This article was first published on LinkedIn. For a deeper dive into this topic and to stay updated with our latest insights,[you can access the full newsletter here.](https://www.linkedin.com/newsletters/ea-market-sparks-7394040447238828032/)

## [Energy Economics](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/energy-economics "Go to Energy Economics on ScienceDirect")

[Volume 150](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/energy-economics/vol/150/suppl/C "Go to table of contents for this volume/issue"), October 2025, 108814

[![Image 6: Energy Economics](https://ars.els-cdn.com/content/image/1-s2.0-S0140988325X00098-cov150h.gif)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/energy-economics/vol/150/suppl/C)

# Geopolitical risks and energy market dynamics

Author links open overlay panel Niraj P.Koirala a, Linus Nyiwul b, Zhining Hu b, Rashid Al-Hmoud c, Dhiroj Prasad Koirala d

Show more

Outline

Add to Mendeley

Share

Cite

[https://doi.org/10.1016/j.eneco.2025.108814](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.eneco.2025.108814 "Persistent link using digital object identifier")[Get rights and content](https://s100.copyright.com/AppDispatchServlet?publisherName=ELS&contentID=S0140988325006413&orderBeanReset=true)

Under a Creative Commons [license](http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc/4.0/)

Open access

## Highlights

*   •Geopolitical risks affect energy consumption non-linearly and asymmetrically. 
*   •Effect of geopolitical risks differs by energy type, trade balance, development status. 
*   •Geopolitical risks reduce energy trade flows. 
*   •Renewable energy growth may be hindered by geopolitical risks in developed economies. 
*   •Coal production increases significantly with a rise in geopolitical risks. 

## Abstract

In this paper, we examine the relationship between geopolitical risks and energy markets, focusing on how these risks impact energy consumption, trade, and the transition to renewable energy. Using the System Generalized Methods of Moments (SYS-GMM) on energy market-related data from the World Bank, we find that geopolitical risks do not have statistically significant impacts on overall energy consumption in our full sample. However, further analyses suggest that geopolitical risks have heterogeneous impacts on overall energy consumption based on the status of development and whether countries are net energy exporters or importers. Specifically, we find that geopolitical risks are associated with increased energy consumption in both developed and developing countries. However, developing countries increase not only the level but also the intensity of consumption, highlighting the need to increase energy stockpiles. This finding remains robust to alternative measurements of geopolitical risks and estimation approaches, such as the Instrumental Variable (IV) method. In addition to energy consumption, we also find that geopolitical risks positively affect renewable energy growth in developed countries while increasing reliance on fossil fuels at the same time. This finding suggests a delicate balance developing countries need to strike while maintaining current energy demands and promoting energy sovereignty by promoting renewable energy at the same time. Lastly, we find that geopolitical risks decrease both energy exports and imports. However, such a shrinkage in energy trade is followed by strong exports and imports signifying a profit seeking behavior of exporter countries and a need to rely on imported fuels to maintain domestic energy security of importers. These findings underscore the importance of geopolitical risks in understanding energy market dynamics.

*   [Previous article in issue](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S014098832500667X)
*   [Next article in issue](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006152)

Q48

## Keywords

Geopolitical risks

Energy consumption

Political stability

Renewable energy

Energy trade

## 1. Introduction

Recent geopolitical developments have served to highlight the significance of uncertainties and their perverse impacts on the global economy. The Russian invasion of Ukraine starting on February 24, 2022 resulted in a 29.1 percent loss of the latter’s gross domestic product (GDP) in 2022 alone ([Reuters, 2023](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b51)), and exacerbated global food insecurities due to agricultural trade dependencies on both Ukraine and Russia ([Hellegers, 2022](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b30)). The October 7, 2023 attack on a concert and the kidnapping of 1,200 people in Israel by Hamas, as well as the subsequent Israeli response contributed to a decline in the country’s consumer confidence, resulting in a reported 19.4 percent decrease in GDP for the fourth quarter of 2023 ([Scheer, 2024](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b54)). Additionally, this led to a rise in regional tensions and the involvement of other regional players, such as the Houthis in Yemen and Hezbollah in Lebanon. Furthermore, on May 19, 2024, the President of Iran, Ibrahim Raisi, died in a helicopter crash, further catalyzing political uncertainty in Iran and tensions in the Middle East in general ([Gerecht, 2024](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b26)). These events, especially the war in Ukraine and the regional nature of the conflict in Palestine, have affected the flow of goods and services in international trade. All the countries involved in the recent geopolitical episodes cited above are either major oil suppliers or located in regions rich in energy resources. Political upheavals in these countries are likely to influence the global supply and demand of energy resources. The above mentioned events highlight the pervasive impacts of geopolitical risks on the global economy. Geopolitical risks reflect disruptive events such as terrorist activities, diplomatic tensions, armed conflicts between countries, etc. These events could lead to instability in financial markets and disruptions in trade flows, making geopolitical uncertainty an important factor to be considered by policymakers and investors when formulating policies and investment plans. In this paper, we focus specifically on the effect of geopolitical tensions on the dynamics of energy supply and demand.

Energy markets and geopolitical events are closely related. The literature on geopolitical uncertainty and energy markets is relatively rich and mainly attempts to explain its impacts on energy price volatilities. For example, [Liu et al. (2021)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b39), utilizing a GARCH-MIDAS model, argue that geopolitical uncertainties amplify energy volatilities. In another case, [Qin et al. (2020)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b48), using the quantile regression, assert that geopolitical uncertainties have significant adverse impacts on oil price returns in a bearish market with no impacts on gas price returns. Regarding the transmission channel, the paper suggests that the impact of geopolitical uncertainties on energy markets is likely to transmit through threats of adverse geopolitical events relative to their realizations. The literature on geopolitical uncertainties also finds that the effect of geopolitical uncertainties on energy price volatilities is asymmetric in nature due to the market’s heightened sensitivity to potential disruptions in energy supply caused by geopolitical tensions, increasing the risk premium ([Qin et al., 2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b48), [Liu et al., 2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b39)).

In addition to energy volatility, geopolitical uncertainty is also reported to affect investment decisions and destinations in the energy sector. For example, [Yu et al. (2021)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b68) find that geopolitical risks around the South China Sea region are shifting Chinese energy investment to less risky regions of the world. Geopolitical risk is also found to influence the transitions towards renewable energy sources (see, for example, [Cheikh and Zaied (2024)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b19) and [Zhao et al. (2024)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b69)). These papers argue that geopolitical risks accelerate the adoption of renewable energy in developed countries, while such transitions do not appear to be promoted in developing and emerging countries. While the literature on geopolitical uncertainties and energy is rich and still evolving, we are yet to understand the dynamics through which energy markets are affected by geopolitical risks. In addition, the literature on the impacts of geopolitical risk on the energy sector and the role of domestic policies in stabilizing geopolitical impacts on the energy sector remains limited. Also, the relationship between geopolitical tensions and energy mix is still a matter of debate in the literature. In this paper, our aim is to fill these gaps in the literature.

This paper makes significant contributions to the literature on energy and geopolitical risks by providing an understanding of how geopolitical risks impact energy markets across different dimensions. First, one of the key contributions to the literature is that we differentiate the effects of geopolitical risks on energy consumption between developed and developing economies. In addition, we assess the effects of geopolitical risks on energy consumption based on whether countries are net energy exporters or importers. The study reveals that geopolitical risks tend to increase energy consumption in both developed and developing countries, reflecting a continued use of energy sources even amid chaos signaling overall stable supply chain issue. However, we find that developing countries increase consumption not only in levels but also in intensity, signaling compounding vulnerabilities in the domestic energy supply chain and heightened domestic energy insecurity. These results align with findings from [Stern (2004a)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b58) and [Bastianin and Manera (2018)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b9) that emphasize the vulnerability of energy demand to shocks in less developed economies.

Second, the paper also finds a differential effect of geopolitical risks on the energy mix. Specifically, it finds that geopolitical risks insignificantly hinder the growth of renewable energy in developed countries, possibly due to supply chain disruptions, particularly in acquiring raw materials and critical minerals which are concentrated in a handful of countries. In addition, countries might focus more on energy security and stability than transitioning to renewable energy. This finding is in contrast with studies by [Sovacool and Mukherjee (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b57) and [York (2012)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b67), which suggest that geopolitical risks might accelerate the transition to renewable energy. However, developing countries see a rise in both fossil fuel and renewable energy consumption. This finding suggests a delicate balance developing countries need to maintain while fulfilling current energy demands and focusing on renewable sources means to secure energy sovereignty.

Third, our paper adds new insights to the literature on international energy trade. Specifically, we find that with a rise in geopolitical risks, there is a decrease in international energy flows in terms of exports and imports. This is potentially linked to supply chain disruptions. However, the paper also finds that trade reductions are followed by statistically significant exports and imports. This is potentially linked to energy-exporting countries’ opportunistic behavior, as geopolitical risks disrupt the energy supply and hence raise energy prices, thus increasing energy exporting countries’ revenue. In addition, by attempting to increase energy exports during geopolitically critical times, energy producers can enhance their geopolitical influence by using energy exports as a tool. However, energy-importing countries might increase energy imports out of caution due to a need to maintain energy security. In addition, energy-importing countries may reduce energy imports as an increase in energy prices increases the burden on their economies. As a result, those countries might focus on domestic energy production. These findings are in line with [Kilian (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b36), [Cherp and Jewell (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b20), and [Baumeister and Peersman (2013)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b11).

Fourth, the paper advances the literature by employing a two-stage least squares (2SLS) instrumental variable regression model, which addresses potential endogeneity issues and captures causal relationships between geopolitical risks and energy demand. The 2SLS approach in this paper uses military spending, gold price, and wheat price as instruments. In addition, we develop a novel instrument of global geopolitical risks based on peace agreements countries have signed. This method builds on the approach of [Arellano and Bond (1991)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b5) and [Arellano and Bover (1995)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b6), providing robust results.

Fifth, we also study the dynamic nature of geopolitical risks. In doing so, we divide the analysis into the Cold War era and the modern era, revealing that the direct impact of geopolitical risks on energy consumption has diminished in recent times due to energy diversification and technological advancements, a finding that resonates with the work of [Stevens (2012)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b61) on the evolving nature of global energy markets. Additionally, the study explores the role of institutional quality and political stability, offering insights into the complex and non-linear relationship between governance and energy market responses to geopolitical uncertainties, as discussed by [Alesina et al. (1996)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b2) and [Povitkina (2018)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b47).

Sixth, the paper delves into sector-specific impacts, examining how geopolitical risks affect fossil fuel use, renewable energy consumption, and electricity access. The study finds that geopolitical risks negatively impact renewable energy use, especially in developed countries, which is consistent with the findings by [Sovacool and Mukherjee (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b57). The analysis of electricity access, particularly in developing countries, is a novel contribution that links geopolitical instability directly to infrastructure development, an area with limited insight in the existing literature.

Finally, the paper assesses the relationships between geopolitical risks and energy production. The paper finds that an increase in geopolitical risk is associated with a significant increase in coal production. This is likely due to the fact that coal-producing countries might ramp up coal production in anticipation of geopolitical risks which influences oil production- though statistically insignificantly.

The rest of the paper proceeds as follows. In Section [2](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#sec2), we explain data and methodology used in the paper. Section [3](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#sec3) reports results of our analysis on the relationship between energy market and geopolitical risks. Section [4](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#sec4) studies the relationship between geopolitical risks and energy from supply side perspectives. Lastly, we conclude the paper with a discussion in Section [5](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#sec5).

## 2. Data and methodology

### 2.1. Data and variables

The paper uses annual data between 1960 and 2023 for 41 countries, sourced from the World Bank Development Indicators (WDI). Since we study how geopolitical uncertainty affects multiple dimensions of energy outcomes, our dependent variables reflect this by considering different characteristics, including total energy use oil equivalent, fossil fuel use, fossil fuel export and import, renewable energy consumption, and electricity access. These variables allow us to assess the long-term effects of geopolitical uncertainty on the dynamics of energy supply and demand.

The main explanatory variable is the historical domestic geopolitical uncertainty index developed by [Caldara and Matteo (2022)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b18). The index is administered through the website of Economic Policy Uncertainty. The index is based on a newspaper tally of articles covering geopolitical tensions. The index reflects automated text results of the electronic archives of 10 newspapers: the Daily Telegraph, Financial Times, the Chicago Tribune, The Globe and Mail, the Guardian, the Los Angeles Times, the New York Times, USA Today, the Wall Street Journal, and the Washington Post. The authors calculate the index by counting the number of articles related to adverse geopolitical events in each newspaper in each month. The geopolitical uncertainty index is available in the monthly frequency from January 1, 1900. In addition to the availability of data at the country level, the index is also available in global level, capturing geopolitical tensions in the world level. [Fig. 1](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fig1) illustrates the Global Geopolitical Uncertainty Index from January 1, 1900. The figure shows several spikes, and each spike corresponds to an event that raises geopolitical tensions. For example, the first spike is observed in April 1986 when the US Air Force launched airstrikes on select targets in Libya in retaliation for that country’s involvement in terrorist incidents in West Berlin. The second spike occurs in August 1990 with Iraq’s invasion of Kuwait and the subsequent Gulf War. The index reaches its peak value after 2001 following the September 11 attacks. In the figure, after the 9/11 event, geopolitical tensions subside. However, the index increases around 2014 when Ukraine was engulfed in political turmoil that increased US-Russia geopolitical tensions. Geopolitical tensions rose again in 2020 in the aftermath of COVID-19 and renewed strain in relations between the U.S. and Iran. Given the nature of the index to increase during times of geopolitical tensions and decrease during peace, we use it as a proxy for geopolitical tensions. This is in line with the increasing use of the index in the literature as a proxy of geopolitical tensions (see, for instance, [Demir and Danisman, 2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b22), [Pata et al., 2023](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b44)).

We control for other effects, including economic situation, industry share in GDP, weather variables, institutional indicators, industry size, and price variables. We use the log difference of GDP per capita in constant 2015 USD from the World Development Indicators for economic growth. The economic growth-led energy hypothesis suggests that as an economy grows, the demand for energy-intensive goods and services rises, leading to rising energy demand (see, for example, [Stern, 2004b](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b59), [Apergis and Payne, 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b4), [Sadorsky, 2011](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b52)). The literature suggests that the relationship between economic growth and energy consumption depends on the share of industry in the economy (see, for example, [Narayan and Smyth, 2008](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b41), [Sorrell, 2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b56), [Nyiwul, 2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b42). The established hypothesis is that the higher the share of industry in a country’s GDP the higher the energy demand. Therefore, we use the share of industry in a country’s GDP as an explanatory variable. Apart from industry’s share of GDP, we also use a weather variable as another independent variable, as it plays an important role in energy demand. For example, [Quayle and Diaz (1980)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b49) argue that there exists a positive relationship between temperature and energy demand. However, [Bessec and Fouquau (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b15) suggest a nonlinearity in this relationship.

Fourth, institutional quality might affect energy demand, as strong institutions and better governance systems help countries formulate effective energy policies that might influence energy demand. We use Polity2 as a proxy for the institutional quality. Polity2 measures the extent of democracy in a country. Democratic governance is conducive to the formulation of energy policies and better regulation through public participation at the grassroots level, influencing energy demand. However, the better aspect of democracy in formulating suitable energy policies might not always translate into less energy demand due to public pressure to improve the economy (see, for example, [Povitkina, 2018](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b47)). Lastly, we also use price variables as determinants of energy demand. Energy price affects demand through substitution effects and income effects. An increase in the price of an energy product might force consumers to use low-quality energy products. The relationship between energy price and energy demand in the name of price elasticity of demand has been a subject of intense study in the literature. For example, [Hamilton (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b28) examines the dynamism of the price elasticity of energy demand. The paper argues that the price elasticity of demand for energy products has been declining in recent times as Americans are purchasing energy products even at higher prices. [Hughes et al. (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b32) estimate that the oil price elasticity of demand to be in the range of −0.21 and −0.34 over 1975–1980 but between −0.034 and −0.072 for 2001–06. Despite the dwindling impact, price variables play an important role in energy demand. Therefore, we use the log difference of the real price of oil and coal as other independent variables in our analysis.

Descriptive statistics of the variables used in this paper are reported in [Table 1](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl1).

The table suggests that developing countries have a significantly high growth rate of total energy use, fossil fuel use, and industry share. Lastly, we observe that developed countries have greater volatility in geopolitical risks compared to developing countries. This disparity may stem from the newspapers used in the construction process of the index.

### 2.2. Empirical framework

In order to explore further the relationships between energy market variables and geopolitical risks, we employ a system generalized method of moments (SYS-GMM) to establish the causal relationships between the variables. The empirical framework according to SYS-GMM assumes the following form: (1)$\Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t} = \beta_{0} + \beta_{1} \Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t - 1} + \beta_{2} \Delta G p r c h_{i , t} + \beta_{3} \Delta y_{i , t} + \beta_{4} \Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t} + \beta_{5} \Delta P o l i t y 2_{i , t} + \beta_{6} \Delta P o l i t y 2 S q_{i , t} + \beta_{7} \Delta C o a l_{t} + \beta_{8} \Delta O i l_{t} + u_{i} + \xi_{t} + \epsilon_{i , t} .$ In Eqs. [(1)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd1), [(2)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd2), _i_ refers to the country and _t_ denotes time. $\Delta$ Gprch is the absolute change in geopolitical risk. The energy variable references the key dimensions of energy considered in this paper, such as energy use oil equivalent, fossil fuel use, fossil fuel exports and imports, renewable energy, and electricity access. The calculation process of major variables is explained below. $\Delta y_{i , t}$ is the growth rate of the real gross domestic product per capita to reflect the purchasing power of people as well as the economic growth. $\Delta$ IndustryShare is the change in the share of industry value added in GDP. $\Delta$ Polity2, $\Delta$ Coal, and $\Delta$ Oil refer to a change in Polity2, the percent change in the real prices of coal and oil, respectively. $u_{i}$ is other interference terms while $\epsilon_{i , t}$ is the residual term. In addition to Eq. [(1)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd1), we also add a non-linear term on Gprch as follows: (2)$\Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t} = \beta_{0} + \beta_{1} \Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t - 1} + \beta_{2} \Delta G p r c h_{i , t} + \beta_{3} \Delta G p r c h 2 S q_{i , t} + \beta_{4} \Delta y_{i , t} + \beta_{5} \Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t} + \beta_{6} \Delta P o l i t y 2_{i , t} + \beta_{7} \Delta P o l i t y 2 S q_{i , t} + \beta_{8} \Delta C o a l_{t} + \beta_{9} \Delta O i l_{t} + u_{i} + \xi_{t} + \epsilon_{i , t} .$$\Delta G p r c h_{i , t} = G p r c h_{i , t} - G p r c h_{i , t - 1}$$\Delta y_{i , t} = l o g \left(\right. G D P C a p i t a_{i , t} \left.\right) - l o g \left(\right. G D P C a p i t a_{i , t - 1} \left.\right)$$\Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t} = I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t} - I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t - 1}$$\Delta P o l i t y 2_{i , t} = P o l i t y 2_{i , t} - P o l i t y 2_{i , t - 1}$$\Delta C o a l_{i , t} = l o g \left(\right. C o a l P r i c e_{i , t} \left.\right) - l o g \left(\right. C o a l P r i c e_{i , t - 1} \left.\right)$$\Delta O i l_{i , t} = l o g \left(\right. O i l P r i c e_{i , t} \left.\right) - l o g \left(\right. O i l P r i c e_{i . t - 1} \left.\right)$$\Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t} = l o g \left(\right. E n e r g y U s e_{i , t} \left.\right) - l o g \left(\right. E n e r g y U s e_{i , t - 1} \left.\right)$

In Eq. [(1)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd1), some of the independent and dependent variables might have comovement due to the same shock, resulting in a potential problem of endogeneity. In order to address such potential issues, we apply the SYS-GMM model as the benchmark model. For that purpose, the SYS-GMM approach uses lagged levels as well as lagged differences as instruments. Also, this method is suitable for data with large number of panels and many missing observations ([Arellano and Bond, 1991](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b5)), as is the case with our data. In addition, the data contain missing values and few observations for many countries outside of the U.S and Europe. The SYS-GMM is considered efficient in comparison to Diff GMM because the latter would use only the lagged levels as instruments, which have been shown to perform poorly [Arellano and Bover, 1995](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b6), [Blundell and Bond, 1998](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b16). In addition to SYS-GMM, we also examine Sargan statistics and perform autocorrelation tests to determine whether the results are consistent and unbiased.

## 3. Results

### 3.1. Geopolitical uncertainties and energy demand

In this subsection, we study the effect of geopolitical risks on energy demand. [Table 2](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl2) presents a set of SYS-GMM estimation results in which the dependent variable is the log difference of oil equivalent energy use. The estimated models include one year lagged energy use and various determinants, including output growth, coal price, oil price, temperature change, geopolitical risks, and political institutions index.

The results in [Table 2](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl2) suggest that the main factors closely associated with energy demand include output growth, coal price, oil price, temperature, and political institutions. As for geopolitical risks, specifications (III) and (IV) suggest the presence of a nonlinear relationship between geopolitical risks and energy demand. In fact, the presence of a negative and significant coefficient of $\Delta G p r c h 2 S q$ in specification IV indicates that an initial increase in geopolitical risk increases energy use non-significantly but reduces it beyond a certain threshold. This may suggest that extreme geopolitical instability prompts energy conservation or a shift to more secure energy sources. As for the political institutions, both the linear and the quadratic coefficients suggest that democratic political institutions are likely to affect energy consumption negatively and significantly. Interestingly, the finding remains robust to all the specifications and is in line with the literature (see, for example, [Li and Reuveny, 2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b38), [Fredriksson and Wollscheid, 2007](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b24)). Such a robust findings suggest that democratic countries are likely to enforce more stringent environmental regulations affecting the overall energy demand. Furthermore, the presence of significant and negative coefficients of the squared terms of Polity2 indicates that the marginal impacts of political institutions on limiting energy demand rises with the increase in democratic practices in a country. This finding is also in agreement with the literature such as [Chou and Zhang (2020)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b21) which argues that a higher degree of democracy is more effective in promoting environmental conservation and energy efficiency. Most importantly, the presence of the significant and negative quadratic term echoes the framework studied by [Alesina et al. (1996)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b2) which stresses that stable and inclusive political systems are likely to induce favorable economic and policy outcomes. Extending that logic to economic growth and environmental dimension, higher degree of democratic practices might provide safer and stable investment environment which accelerate progress towards energy efficient economy, thereby reducing energy demand. Lastly, the lack of significant impact from the industry share on energy use could reflect the transition in many economies toward service-oriented sectors, reducing the energy intensity of industrial production, a trend noted by [Wing (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b64).

#### 3.1.1. Geopolitical risks and energy use: Heterogeneity analysis

The previous section suggested a possible presence of a non-linear effect of geopolitical risks and political institutions on energy demand. However, the full sample includes countries with vastly different characteristics. Therefore, it is logical to explore further to better understand the nature of impacts of geopolitical risks on energy demand. In this subsection, we first divide the total sample into developed countries and developing countries and rerun the analysis. In the second case, we divide the total sample into net energy-importing countries and energy-exporting countries. Countries are classified as net importers or exporters based on the status of energy export/import data obtained from Our World in Data. In this subsection, we attempt to study the energy demand based on these sub-classifications of our data.

#### 3.1.2. Heterogeneity analysis based on development status of countries

In this subsection, we study the impact of geopolitical risks on energy demand based on the development status of countries. [Table 3](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl3) reports the results. The regression analysis investigates the influence of geopolitical risks on energy consumption, differentiated by development status, and connects these findings to the associated literature. In developed countries, economic growth ($\Delta y_{t}$) shows a strong positive and statistically significant association with energy consumption, with a coefficient of 0.57, indicating that economic expansion drives energy use. This aligns with the findings of [Stern (2004a)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b58), who noted the crucial link between GDP growth and energy demand in developed economies. Temperature changes ($\Delta T e m p_{t}$) have a significant negative effect (coefficient $=$ −0.59), suggesting that higher temperatures reduce energy consumption and is consistent with [Auffhammer and Aroonruengsawat (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b8) who highlighted the role of climate in shaping energy demand patterns. Geopolitical risks ($\Delta G p r h c_{t}$) are positively associated with energy consumption (coefficient $=$ 24.712, significant at the 5% level), possibly due to the increased need to secure energy supplies during periods of uncertainty, a concept supported by [Klare (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b37) who discusses the geopolitical dimensions of energy security. Industry share ($\Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{t}$) is also positively correlated with energy use (coefficient $=$ 0.37, significant at the 1% level), reflecting the energy-intensive nature of industrial activities in high-income countries, as noted by [Wing (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b64).

In contrast, for developing countries, economic growth ($\Delta y_{t}$) remains a key driver of energy consumption, with a statistically significant coefficient of 0.54, corroborating the findings of [Ang (2007)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b3) that emphasize the role of economic growth in energy demand in less developed economies. Unlike in developed countries, temperature changes do not significantly affect energy consumption, indicating different dynamics in climatic and energy use. Both the linear and the squared terms of geopolitical risk ($\Delta G p r c h 2 S q_{t}$ shows strong positive and statistically significant coefficients, suggesting that with the rise in geopolitical risks, developing countries do not increase energy consumption only in level but also in intensity. The larger value of coefficients on the squared term suggests compounding vulnerabilities in energy systems caused by higher level of political instability, military operations, or inefficiencies in energy supply chain. Such a finding is also in line with the literature (see, for example, [Klare, 2008](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b37)). Industry share does not significantly influence energy consumption in developing countries, highlighting the distinct economic structures and lower energy intensity compared to developed nations.

#### 3.1.3. Geopolitical risks and energy consumption based on energy import and export

In this subsection, we study the effect of geopolitical risks on energy demand based on whether a country in the sample is an energy exporter or importer. [Table 4](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl4) reports the results of this analysis. The dependent variable in both cases is the change in total energy use.

For net importer countries, economic growth ($\Delta y_{t}$) is positively and significantly associated with energy consumption, with a coefficient of 0.58, indicating that as these economies grow, their energy demand increases. This finding aligns with the literature, such as [Ang (2007)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b3), which emphasizes the strong link between economic expansion and energy consumption, especially in countries reliant on energy imports. Lagged energy use ($\Delta E n e r g y U s e \left(\right. t - 1 \left.\right)$) shows a significant negative effect (−0.22), suggesting that past energy consumption levels influence current energy use, possibly due to adjustments in energy efficiency or shifts to alternative energy sources. Temperature changes ($\Delta T e m p t_{t}$) have a significant negative effect (−0.43), consistent with the findings of [Auffhammer and Aroonruengsawat (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b8), which indicate that higher temperatures can lead to reduced energy consumption, possibly due to decreased heating demand. The coefficient (0.004) on the geopolitical risk variable, $\Delta G p r c h_{t}$, is positive. The positive coefficient might be suggesting that increased geopolitical uncertainty leads to higher energy consumption in net importer countries, potentially due to concerns about energy security, as highlighted by [Klare (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b37). The negative sign on the coefficient of the squared term of geopolitical risk ($\Delta G p r c h 2 S q_{t}$) further suggests a non-linear relationship, where after a certain threshold, increased risks may lead to reduced energy use, possibly due to market disruptions or changes in energy sourcing strategies. However, these findings should be interpreted with caution due to statistical significance.

For energy exporting countries, the results differ notably. Economic growth, $\Delta y_{t}$ remains a positive and statistically significant driver of energy consumption, with a coefficient of 0.45, but the impact is less pronounced than in net importer countries. The effect of temperature changes is statistically insignificant and smaller, indicating that while temperature might influence energy use positively, its impact is less critical compared to net importer countries. As in the case of net importers, geopolitical risk $\Delta G p r c h_{t}$ is not statistically significant, and the squared term $\Delta G p r h c 2 S q_{t}$ does not show a statistically significant effect, suggesting that geopolitical risks do not have the immediate impact on energy consumption in exporting countries as in energy-importing countries, possibly due to their more secure energy supplies or different economic structures. Industry share, $\Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{t}$, has a positive and statistically significant relationship with energy use in net importer countries, but does not have a significant effect in energy exporting countries, indicating that the industrial composition of energy use varies significantly depending on the country’s status as an importer or exporter.

These findings in [Table 4](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl4) attempt to provide an understanding of how geopolitical risks and other factors influence energy consumption differently based on whether a country is an energy importer or exporter. The results suggest that domestic geopolitical risks might influence energy consumption differently depending upon whether the countries are energy exporters or importers. To be specific, geopolitical risks might increase energy consumption in net energy importer countries while energy demand might be reduced at higher level of risks. In contrast, for energy exporters, geopolitical risks might initially decrease energy demand while increasing later, possibly due to domestic redirection of energy flows or intensified extraction of new energy sources during crises. However, the presence of insignificant coefficients requires a further analysis on the issue. The model diagnostics, including the Wald $\chi^{2}$, Sargan, and AR(2) tests, indicate that results presented in the Table are econometrically robust across both groups of countries.

### 3.2. Robustness analysis

#### 3.2.1. Effect of geopolitical risks on energy consumption under alternative specification

In this subsection, we further explore the robustness of our findings. Specifically, we study the effects of geopolitical risks on energy consumption in alternative specifications. We first use a three-year moving average of the level variables of geopolitical risks and Polity2. This allows us to capture the sustained impact of geopolitical risks and political stability over the sample period, smoothing out the short-term fluctuations. [Table 5](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl5) reports the results of the analysis. The results reveal starkly different outcomes especially in the case of developing countries when compared to the analyses above. Specifically, we find that the squared term of geopolitical risks have significant and negative impacts on energy consumption in developing countries. This finding suggests that initially geopolitical risks increase energy demand in developing countries due to panic stockpiling or precautionary demand, but decline in the medium term due to weak infrastructure, political instability, and economic contraction. Another important distinction that appears in [Table 5](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl5) is with regards to the impact of Polity2. When using the three year moving averages of the institutional variable, significance of both level and quadratic variables are almost lost, suggesting that smoothened variables reduce the within country variation required for identification in dynamic panel models. This highlights a potential trade-off resulting from smoothing: while the technique helps us understand the non-linear impacts of volatile variables like geopolitical risks in the medium term, it may remove the effect of slowly changing institutional variables like Polity2.

#### 3.2.2. Global geopolitical risks and energy demand

In this subsection, we assess the impacts of global geopolitical risk on the same energy variables as in the previous section. Global geopolitical risks account for geopolitical events that affect multiple countries and cause significant uncertainty in the global economy. Events such as the US-China trade war or Gulf wars affect the global economy by influencing the supply and demand of goods and energy supply. The calculation process of global geopolitical risks includes such events. This is in strict contrast to the domestic geopolitical risks used previously, which have been localized within a country or specific region, with the civil war in Nepal being an example of it. The global geopolitical risk indices are constructed by analyzing the frequency of newspaper articles covering geopolitical tensions globally, while country-specific geopolitical risks are calculated using country-specific newspapers. [Table 6](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl6) reports the results of the effects of global geopolitical risks on energy consumption.

As in the case of country-specific geopolitical risks, increasing global geopolitical risks insignificantly affect energy use. In particular, a rise in global geopolitical risks by one unit decrease energy consumption in the whole sample by 0.003% which is lower than results reported in previous specifications. However, the heterogeneity analysis suggests a negative but insignificant impact on developed and developing countries, perhaps due to the sampling effect. In addition, the use of global geopolitical risks seems insignificant in both the developed and developing countries, again, perhaps due to the sampling effect or endogeneity.

#### 3.2.3. Effect of geopolitical risks on energy consumption: an IV approach

Previous analyses gave mixed results on the impacts of geopolitical risks, perhaps due to the endogeneity issue as it could be that both the energy variable and geopolitical variables might be subjected to the same economic shocks. Therefore, it is logical to study the relationship using an external instrument. In this subsection, we do that by using an instrumental variable (IV) method. Ideal instruments should affect geopolitical uncertainty. However, the instrument should affect the energy variables through geopolitical uncertainty. Based on the literature in economics, we choose military spending as a fraction of GDP as an instrument for geopolitical risk. The reason behind the use of the instrument lies in the assumption that an increase in military spending stabilizes regional and global security, helping to reduce geopolitical risks. In addition, a rise in military spending sends signals to rivals and allies of countries about the possible outcomes of geopolitical escalations and inducing countries to be prudent in strategic planning and actions. These views are well supported by the literature (see, for example, ([Goldstein, 2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b27), [Smith and Dunne, 2020](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b55), [Gartzke, 2017](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b25))). Another reason for the selection of military spending as an instrument is that it is unlikely that military spending alone would have implications on energy issues by itself without considering the geopolitical channel through which it influences energy supply, demand, and transformation. This assumption is backed by recent data on the energy consumption of the Department of Defense (DoD) of the United States. The US military expenditures account for about 37% of the total global military expenditure ([SIPRI, 2023](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b62)), highlighting the potential to change the international security architecture. Although the Department of Defense is one of the largest single energy consumers in the world, its relative share of total US energy consumption remains low, as shown in [Fig. 2](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fig2) below. The figure suggests that DoD’s relative energy consumption has been constantly decreasing since the 1970s. For instance, at its peak, the DoD consumed about 1.5% of total energy in 1975 and since then it is decreasing persistently, reaching its nadir at 0.48% in 2023. These facts suggest that higher military spending may not always translate into higher energy consumption, which reduces the risk of endogeneity. Furthermore, these data suggest that military spending might primarily capture the geopolitical crisis rather than energy demand, make it an excellent instrument for geopolitical risks.

Data on military spending are collected from the World Development Indicator (WDI) in the World Bank Group database. The price of wheat is the other variable that we use as an instrument. Wheat prices have been identified as a strong instrument for geopolitical risks due to their sensitivity to global political instability, particularly in regions that are heavily dependent on wheat imports, such as the Middle East and North Africa. Research has shown that geopolitical events, such as conflicts or trade embargoes, can significantly disrupt wheat supply chains, leading to sharp fluctuations in wheat prices ([Bellemare, 2015](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b14), [Hendrix and Brinkman, 2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b31)). These price changes, while influenced by geopolitical instability, are often exogenous to the specific economic outcomes being studied, making wheat prices a relevant instrument.

![Image 8](https://ars.els-cdn.com/content/image/1-s2.0-S0140988325006413-gr2.jpg)
1.   [Download: Download high-res image (196KB)](https://ars.els-cdn.com/content/image/1-s2.0-S0140988325006413-gr2_lrg.jpg "Download high-res image (196KB)")
2.   [Download: Download full-size image](https://ars.els-cdn.com/content/image/1-s2.0-S0140988325006413-gr2.jpg "Download full-size image")

Fig. 2. US Department of Defense Energy Consumption as a Ratio of Total US Energy Consumption.

•_Notes:_ Energy consumption includes direct DoD operations across all sectors. Data sourced from the U.S. Energy Information Administration (EIA) Annual Energy Review. Percentages are derived from total U.S. energy consumption, including residential, commercial, industrial, transportation, electric power, and government sectors.

In addition, we also use the gold price as a possible instrument for geopolitical risks. In the economics and finance literature, gold has been described as a safe haven asset. When geopolitical risks increase, investors typically flock to gold as a store of value, driving its price upward. This behavior is well documented in the literature (see, for example, [Baur and Lucey, 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b12)). Although gold prices are influenced by financial market volatility and changes in investor behavior due to geopolitical risks, they are not influenced by the same factors that drive energy demand, such as temperature, industry share, output growth, and technological progress (see, for example, [Reboredo, 2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b50)). Following the arguments made in previous paragraphs, we assume that the instrumental variables we consider here positively affect geopolitical risks. Thus, we next adopt a two-stage regression framework that can be expressed as follows: (3)$\Delta G P R_{t} = \alpha_{i} + \alpha_{1} \Delta M i l i t a r y S p e n d i n g_{i , t} + \alpha_{2} \Delta W h e a t P r i c e_{t} + \alpha_{2} \Delta G o l d P r i c e_{t} + \nu_{i} + \epsilon_{i , t}$(4)$\Delta E n e r g y U s e_{i , t} = \beta_{0} + \beta_{1} \overset{̂}{\Delta G P R_{t}} + \beta_{2} \Delta y_{i , t} + \beta_{3} \Delta I n d u s t r y S h a r e_{i , t} + \beta_{4} \Delta P o l i t y 2_{i , t} + \beta_{5} \Delta P o l i t y 2 S q_{i , t} + \beta_{6} \Delta C o a l_{t} + \beta_{7} \Delta O i l_{t} + u_{i} + \epsilon_{i , t} .$

Eqs. [(3)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd3), [(4)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd4) represent the first-stage and second-stage equations. [Table 12](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl12) reports the results of Eqs. [(3)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd3), [(4)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#fd4).

[Table 7](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl7) provides important insights on the relationship between geopolitical risks and energy consumption. The geopolitical risk coefficient ($\Delta \text{Gpr}_{t}$) is positive but statistically insignificant (0.0004), indicating that changes in geopolitical risk do not have a direct and significant impact on the growth of energy use in this model. This finding aligns with recent literature, such as the work of Caldara and Iacoviello (2018), which suggests that while geopolitical risks can disrupt financial markets and influence energy prices, their direct effects on real economic activities like energy consumption may be complex and mediated through other factors.
