Interestingly, the model shows a statistically significant negative relationship between military expenditure ($\Delta \text{MilitaryExpenditure}_{t}$) and geopolitical risk, with a coefficient of $- 1 . 33$. This suggests that increased military spending is associated with a reduction in geopolitical risks, supporting theories of deterrence and strategic signaling. The literature by [Gartzke (2017)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b25) corroborates this finding, highlighting how military investments can stabilize regions by deterring conflicts and projecting power, thus reducing the perceived level of geopolitical risk.

The instruments used in the model, specifically wheat (with coefficient of 2.40) and gold price (with a coefficient of 4.07), are statistically significant, confirming their effectiveness in capturing variations in geopolitical risk. This is consistent with the literature, such as [Baur and Lucey (2010)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b12), which discusses how gold prices increase during times of geopolitical uncertainty, and [Hendrix and Brinkman (2013)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b31), who explore how food prices like wheat are impacted by geopolitical instability.

In terms of control variables, GDP growth ($\Delta y_{t}$) is positively associated with energy use, reflecting the expected relationship between economic activity and energy consumption. In contrast, higher temperatures ($\Delta \text{Temp}_{t}$) are associated with a reduction in energy use, which could be due to decreased heating demand. Overall, the model is well-identified, with strong instruments and valid overidentification restrictions, as indicated by the diagnostic tests.

#### 3.2.4. Peace agreement as an alternative instrument

In the previous section, we used the price of wheat, military expenditures, and gold prices as instruments for geopolitical risks. The use of those instruments, as shown in the benchmark model framework, suggested that geopolitical risks might have insignificant but positive impacts on energy consumption. To verify the findings of the above IV approach, we now use another instrument. The peace_dummy is a binary variable coded as 1 for years in which a country experienced a major internal or external conflict-ending event, such as a peace agreement, democratic transition, or institutional reconciliation process. It includes both domestic conflicts (e.g., civil war, insurgencies) and international conflicts if their resolution had a clear and direct impact on domestic political or security stability. Otherwise, it is coded as 0.

Specifically, we design a binary variable that addresses peace events, such as a peace agreement, democratic transition, or institutional reconciliation processes. The dummy variable mostly includes domestic events. However, in some cases, we also use international agreements that had profound impacts in restoring peace internationally. Some of the examples of international events considered include the Minsk II agreements and the Sino-British joint declaration. The binary variable assumes a value of 1 in a year in a country if there is a peace accord and zero otherwise. The rationale for the use of the peace agreement dummy as an instrument is based on recent discussions on the importance of the peace process. For example, [Hampson (2006)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b29) suggests that peace processes change the political landscape of countries by reducing geopolitical risks. Similarly, [Pospisil (2019)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b46) argues that formal peace accords bring institutional and political stability in a country. These arguments suggest that peace accords might affect geopolitical uncertainty without having a clear impact on energy consumption. In addition to the peace accord dummy, we use the wheat price as an additional instrument. [Table 8](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl8) reports the results of the IV regression with the new instrument.

[Table 8](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl8) suggests that geopolitical risks have statistically significant and positive impacts on energy consumption. From the perspective of the direction of the relationship, the finding is in line with the benchmark model and the IV method in the previous section. However, the difference in significance could be due to a few reasons. For example, instruments such as the price of wheat and peace accords are closely related to economic activities that affect energy demand, such as precautionary usage, infrastructure reconstruction, new construction, and supply chain development arising from renewed optimism due to the peace process. In contrast, instruments such as the military budget might have delayed impacts on energy consumption.

Lastly, institutional factors such as Polity2 appear to have insignificant impacts as in the baseline model. Regarding the diagnostic tests of the new instruments, we can see that the new instruments pass all the statistical tests, making them valid instrument in our case.

### 3.3. Changing effects of geopolitical risks on energy consumption

In this subsection, we study the effects of geopolitical risks on energy demand by dividing the total sample periods into two distinct eras: the Cold War period (before the 2000s) and the modern era, or the post-Cold War era (after the 2000s). During the Cold War period, geopolitical risks were defined by superpower rivalries and regional conflicts that had a direct impact on energy supply and demand. For example, Soviet Union control over energy sources in Eastern Europe played a pivotal role in geopolitical tensions at that time. During that time, events like the oil embargo of the 1970s and the Iran-Iraq war showed how regional tensions affect energy supply and demand.

The post-Cold War era has been characterized by the rise of a multipolar world where the rise of emerging countries such as China and India, and technological advancements such as the shale revolution in the United States have altered the dynamics of energy markets. In addition, the rise of renewable energy and electric vehicles has the potential to completely shift the existing energy dynamics in this modern era. Given this background, it is important to study whether geopolitical risks affect energy demand dynamics differently in these two eras. [Table 9](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl9) reports the results of our estimation of this relationship for the two eras. The table highlights the changing effects of various factors on energy use across two distinct periods: the Cold War era (before 2000) and the modern era (2000–2023), with a particular emphasis on the role of geopolitical risks. The coefficients associated with economic output, $\Delta y_{t}$, indicate a strong positive relationship with energy use in both periods, with statistically significant coefficients of 0.53 before 2000 and 0.48 after 2000, suggesting that economic growth remains a significant driver of energy consumption, although its influence has decreased slightly in the modern era.

The analysis also shows a notable shift in the impact of coal prices $\Delta C o a l_{t}$. Before 2000, the coefficient for coal prices (−0.001) is statistically insignificant, but for the modern era, it is positive and statistically significant at the 1% level, reflecting a growing sensitivity of energy use to coal prices. Oil prices $\Delta O i l_{t}$, on the other hand, have little direct impact on energy use, with small and statistically insignificant coefficients in both periods (−0.0024 before 2000 and 0.0025 after 2000).

Temperature changes $\Delta T e m p_{t}$ consistently have a negative and significant impact on energy use, with statistically significant coefficients of −0.28 before 2000 and −0.23 after 2000, indicating a reduction in energy demand in response to warmer temperatures, possibly due to lower heating needs.

A central focus of this analysis is the changing impact of geopolitical risks on energy use. During the Cold War, geopolitical risks had a substantial effect on energy supply, as evidenced by the large and statistically significant coefficient of 19.49 for the change in the square of geopolitical risk $\Delta G p r c h 2 S q_{t}$. This period, characterized by intense superpower rivalries and strategic control over energy resources, saw geopolitical tensions significantly disrupt global energy markets. The 1973 OPEC oil embargo and the Iran-Iraq War (1980–1988) are prime examples of how geopolitical events led to substantial energy supply disruptions. Our results here align with the findings of [Yergin (2006)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b65).

However, in the modern era, the direct impact of geopolitical risks on energy use has diminished. The coefficient of change in the square of geopolitical risk $\Delta G p r c h 2 S q_{t}$ drops to −0.22 and becomes statistically insignificant, indicating that although geopolitical events still occur, their influence on overall energy consumption has diminished. This shift can be attributed to the diversification of energy sources, technological advancements, and the rise of new energy players such as China and India, as discussed by [Stevens (2010)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b60). These changes have collectively reduced the vulnerability of global energy supplies to geopolitical disruptions.

### 3.4. Geopolitical risks and policy measures

In the previous sections, we found that geopolitical risks have heterogeneous impacts on energy consumption. In this section, we study whether strong policy measures are able to nullify some of the negative impacts of geopolitical risks on the energy market. Fuel subsidies are one of the most important policy tools that countries have been using to boost the production and consumption of energy products around the world. One of the arguments for implementing fuel subsidies in adverse situations such as those caused by geopolitical risks is price stabilization. By implementing subsidy programs, governments can stabilize domestic fuel prices that are vulnerable to price volatility resulting from global tensions ([Arezki and Blanchard, 2014](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b7)). In addition, fuel subsidies, especially on renewable sources during times of adverse geopolitical developments, can help maintain energy security ([Baumeister and Kilian, 2016](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b10)).

[Table 10](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl10) reports the effects of fuel subsidies on energy demand. The table provides valuable insights on the effectiveness of energy subsidies in boosting energy use. The coefficients for the change in subsidies ($\Delta \text{Subsidies}_{t}$) are positive and statistically significant in both models, indicating that an increase in subsidies is associated with a corresponding increase in energy consumption. Specifically, the results suggest that for each unit increase in subsidies, energy use increases by approximately 0.026 to 0.025 units, which underscores the effectiveness of subsidies as a policy tool to enhance energy consumption. Furthermore, the statistical significance of these coefficients implies that this relationship is robust and is unlikely to be due to random variation.

However, the interaction term between subsidies and geopolitical risks ($\Delta \text{Subsidies}_{t} \times \Delta \text{Gprhc}_{t}$) does not show a statistically significant effect. This suggests that while subsidies independently promote energy use, their effectiveness is not significantly influenced by the level of geopolitical risks. In other words, the positive impact of subsidies on energy consumption remains consistent regardless of geopolitical conditions.

Overall, the analysis indicates that subsidies are an effective mechanism for increasing energy use and their impact is stable across different levels of geopolitical risk. This finding highlights the importance of subsidies as a reliable tool for policymakers aiming to boost energy consumption, even in the face of external geopolitical uncertainties.

### 3.5. Geopolitical risks and effects on different energy sources

Previous sections show that geopolitical risks might negatively influence total energy demand. In addition, our preceding analysis suggests that geopolitical risks might affect developed and developing countries heterogeneously. To further explore this heterogeneity, in this subsection we attempt to study the effects of geopolitical risks on demand for different types of energy use. Here, different types of energy consumption include fossil fuel and renewable energy use. Our main hypothesis is that with an elevation in geopolitical risks, countries could increase the use of fossil fuel energy while, at the same time, investing more in renewable energy and increasing its consumption.

The results are reported in [Table 11](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl11) below. The table indicates that economic growth significantly increases fossil fuel consumption, aligning with [Sadorsky (2014)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b53), who emphasizes the strong link between economic expansion and higher energy demand. Specifically, the table suggests that an increase in economic growth by 1% raises fossil fuel by 0.069%. The positive and statistically significant effect of coal prices on fossil fuel use reflects the inelastic demand for coal, consistent with findings from the IEA (2020), while the negative impact of oil prices suggests a substitution effect, echoing [Hamilton (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b28). In addition, we find that temperature reduces the use of fossil fuels. Specifically, we find that a percentage increase in temperature decreases the share of fossil fuel use in total energy by 0.03%. This is likely due to decreased heating needs, which is supported by [Mansur et al. (2008)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b40). However, the results presented above should be interprted with extreme caution as the model fails to pass AR(2) test.

In contrast, renewable energy consumption is negatively affected by economic growth. [Table 11](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl11) suggests that an increase in output by 1% decreases the share of renewable energy in total energy consumption by 0.039%, and the coefficient is statistically significant at the 1% level. As we previously found that an increase in economic growth increases fossil fuel use, countries might find it convenient to continue relying on fossil fuel use, as suggested in [York (2012)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b67).

As for geopolitical risk, we find that geopolitical risk has insignificant but positive impacts on fossil fuel use, suggesting that fossil fuels are less responsive to shocks. This may be because fossil fuel energy systems are already well established across the world and continuing use of such energy might be cheaper due to sunk costs or path dependency. In contrast, the model with the renewable energy sources suggest that geopolitical risks after certain threshold continue hindering its use. Such outcomes could be because renewable energy sources are still in the development phase and countries might want to limit costly renewable energy production projects to save money in unstable periods. This finding is in line with the literature (see, for example, [Sovacool and Mukherjee, 2011](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b57), [Bremmer, 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b17)). This situation calls for a greater focus on developing the renewable energy sector in developing countries and providing a conducive and supportive policy environment for both private and public initiatives. In this light, innovation is likely to be a key factor ([Nyiwul and Koirala, 2024](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b43)). Such finding is robust from econometric perspectives as Sargan Test and AR(2) test values are insignificant.

In order to understand the detailed impacts of geopolitical risks on energy sources, it becomes logical to study the impacts based on countries’ level of development. In [Table 12](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl12), we present the results on the impacts of geopolitical risks on energy mix according to the development status of countries. The table suggests that in developed countries, past renewable energy use significantly reduces current renewable energy growth, as evidenced by the negative coefficient of 0.25, signaling market saturation or diminishing returns, as noted in the literature by [Unruh (2000)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b63) which argues that developed countries have been locked into fossil fuel-based energy systems. Geopolitical risks also negatively impact the use of renewable energy in developed countries aligning with [Sovacool and Mukherjee (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b57), highlighting the vulnerability of renewable energy investments to global uncertainties. For fossil fuel use in developed countries, the coefficients on economic growth, coal prices, and geopolitical risks are not statistically significant, suggesting a more stable and less sensitive relationship in these regions.

| Empty Cell |  | (0.043) |  | (0.052) |
| Observations | 412 | 461 | 478 | 541 |
| Group | 19 | 19 | 21 | 21 |
| Instruments | 35 | 39 | 35 | 37 |
| Wald $\chi^{2}$ | 642.88 *** | 873.87 *** | 115.32 *** | 522.25 *** |
| Sargan Test | 6.13 | 12.57 | 12.49 | 7.78 |
| AR(2) | −0.099 | −1.41 | −1.604 | −1.238 |

Standard errors in parentheses.

$p < 0 . 10$, ** $p < 0 . 05$, *** $p < 0 . 01$.

In developing countries, the dynamics differ significantly. Past fossil fuel use strongly predicts current fossil fuel consumption, indicating inertia and continued reliance on fossil fuels, consistent with the findings by [Sadorsky (2014)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b53) on energy demand in developing economies. Economic growth also significantly increases fossil fuel use, reflecting the energy-intensive nature of industrialization. In addition, higher coal prices further drive fossil fuel use, suggesting an inelastic demand for coal in these regions, as highlighted by the International Energy Agency IEA (2020). The positive and statistically significant coefficient of 1.124 on $\Delta G p r c h$ for developing countries suggests that geopolitical risks significantly increase fossil fuel use, which may be due to stockpiling or heightened energy security concerns, in line with [Bremmer](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b17)’s ([2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b17)) discussion on energy security. While the developing countries increase reliance on fossil fuel use, these countries also attempt to increase renewable energy consumption, as evidenced by the presence of positive and significant coefficient on Gprch. This is perhaps due to the developing countries’ quest to acquire energy sovereignty in the face of crisis as shown by recent actions by countries such as China in clean energy promotion. However, the use of renewable energy in developing countries is negatively impacted by economic growth, which could be indicative of a continued preference for fossil fuels during periods of economic expansion, as discussed by [York (2012)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b67). These findings underscore the different energy consumption patterns between developed and developing countries, with developing regions showing greater sensitivity to economic growth and geopolitical risks, particularly in their dependence on fossil fuels.

### 3.6. Geopolitical risks and international fuel flows

Previous sections showed that geopolitical risks affect energy mixes within countries. In this section, we investigate whether geopolitical risks affect cross-border energy flows. To do this, we estimate the impacts of geopolitical risks on energy flows in the international market. The results are reported in [Table 13](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl13). The table reveals a non-linear relationship between geopolitical risk and fuel trade dynamics, with geopolitical risk in level having a negative and statistically significant impact on both fuel imports and exports, while the squared term has positive and significant effects on energy flow. This result suggests that countries reduce both imports and exports of fuel due to increased uncertainty, disturbed supply chains, and risk averse behavior of governments and traders. However, at higher levels of geopolitical risks, the countries adapt possibly due to strategic agreements or rerouting the trade to bypass the conflict zones. The positive effect on fuel exports after a brief interruption suggests that during periods of heightened geopolitical risk, major fuel-producing countries may increase their exports to capitalize on higher market prices and meet increasing demand from countries facing supply disruptions. This aligns with the insights provided by [Kilian (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b36), who discusses how oil-exporting nations often exploit global supply shocks to maximize revenues. Additionally, these exporting countries can increase their fuel exports to boost their economies in anticipation of future instability, thereby leveraging temporary surges in global energy prices. In contrast, the non-linear impacts as observed in the case of fuel importing countries suggest that fuel importing countries initially reduce fuel imports due to less control over fuel supply chain and other logistical constraints. However, such behavior is followed by increased imports due energy security or purchase from new partnerships. These behaviors are in line with the explanation presented in [Baumeister and Peersman (2013)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b11) and [Cherp and Jewell (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b20).

The short term fluctuation observed during the Houthi Blockade of the Red Sea in 2023–2025 present an interesting example of these dynamics. The blockade initially increased the volatility in fuel market in terms of supply and price. However, countries kept on maintaining energy flow using alternative routes to expand existing fuel reserves. Such a real life example supports the evidence of a non-linear relationship between international energy trade and geopolitical risks. Although the Table presents compelling results on geopolitical risks and international energy flows, the specification on fuel export suffers from autocorrelation as evidenced by AR(2) test statistic. In order to do robustness check on that, we now use another proxy of geopolitical risks captured by the share of articles related to geopolitical risks in countries without normalizing, Gprc, and rerun the analyses. The results are reported in [Table 14](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl14). The new analysis mostly reaffirms the findings from the previous analysis that geopolitical risks might have non-linear impacts on international energy trade flows.

Table 13. Geopolitical risks and international energy flows.

### 3.7. Geopolitical uncertainty and electricity access

In the previous sections, we established that geopolitical risks affect fossil fuel consumption, renewable energy consumption, and energy trade. These effects of geopolitical risks on energy consumption and trade also have implications for the development of energy infrastructure and, consequently, energy access. Higher access to electricity is an important determinant of economic growth. The economics literature is rich on the relationship between electricity access and economic development (see, for example, [Yoo and Lee, 2010](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b66), [Iyke, 2015](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b33), [Acheampong et al., 2021](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b1)). These papers agree that electricity is crucial for economic development and recommend policies that improve the generation and consumption of electricity to enhance economic growth. The rise in geopolitical tensions such as the Russia–Ukraine war could deter investors and divert investments away from electricity infrastructure. In addition, electricity is produced from coal, renewable energy, natural gas, oil, etc, and as established in the preceding analysis of this paper, these resources are vulnerable to geopolitical risks, likely affecting electricity access in many countries. In this subsection, we attempt to quantify the impacts of geopolitical risks on electricity access for the whole sample as well as sub-samples of developed and developing countries.

[Table 15](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl15) reports the results on the impacts of geopolitical risks on electricity access. The table provides valuable insights on the factors that influence changes in electricity access, ($\Delta E l e c t r i c i t y A c c e s s$), for the whole sample, as well as sub-samples of developed and developing countries. The analysis highlights the varying impacts of economic growth, energy prices (coal and oil), temperature changes, political stability, and geopolitical risks on electricity access. The lagged dependent variable, $\Delta E l e c t r i c i t y A c c e s s \left(\right. t - 1 \left.\right)$ exhibits a strongly negative and statistically significant coefficient in the total sample and sub-samples, with values of $- 0 . 4 1^{* * *}$ for the total sample, $- 0 . 3 9^{* * *}$ developing countries, and $- 0 . 2 3^{* * *}$ for developed countries. This indicates a regression to the mean effect, where significant increases in electricity access in one period are typically followed by smaller gains or reductions in the next. The more pronounced effect in developing countries suggests greater volatility or challenges in sustaining improvements in electricity access, which can be attributed to infrastructural and policy instability in these countries ([Bazillian et al., 2012](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b13)).

Table 15. Geopolitical risks and electricity access.

| Variables | Total sample | Developed countries | Developing countries |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| $\Delta$ ElectricityAccess$_{t - 1}$ | $- 0 . 4 1^{* * *}$ (0.0002) | $- 0 . 2 3^{* * *}$ (0.047) | $- 0 . 3 9^{* * *}$ (0.009) |
| $\Delta y_{t}$ | $0 . 00 9^{* * *}$ (0.001) | 0.00002 (0.0001) | 0.01 (0.009) |
| $\Delta$ Coal$_{t}$ | $0 . 000 4^{* * *}$ (0.0001) | −0.00001 (0.000011) | −0.0003 (0.001) |
| $\Delta$ Oil$_{t}$ | $0 . 000 2^{*}$ (0.0001) | −0.00001 (0.00001) | −0.0003 (0.001) |
| $\Delta$ Temp$_{t}$ | $- 0 . 00 3^{* * *}$ (0.001) | −0.00001 (0.00002) | 0.013 (0.015) |
| $\Delta$ Polity2$_{t}$ | $- 0 . 2 1^{* * *}$ (0.005) | $- 0 . 00 1^{* * *}$ (0.0002) | $- 0 . 1 2^{* * *}$ (0.04) |
| $\Delta$ Polity2Sq$_{t}$ | $0 . 00 7^{* * *}$ (0.0004) | $- 0 . 000 5^{* * *}$ (0.0001) | $0 . 009 9^{* * *}$ (0.002) |

| Observations | 938 | 461 | 477 |
| Number of Groups | 40 | 19 | 21 |
| Instruments | 37 | 24 | 37 |
| Sargan Test | 33.78 | 1.86 | 9.76 |
| AR(2) | −1.36 | −1.32 | $- 1 . 8 6^{*}$ |
| Wald $\chi^{2}$ | $1 . 87 \times 1 0^{11} * * *$ | $284526 . 4 4^{* * *}$ | $189119 . 7 6^{* * *}$ |

_Notes:_ Robust standard errors are in parentheses. *, **, and *** indicate significance at the 10%, 5%, and 1% levels, respectively. The dependent variable is the change in electricity access (percentage of population).

Economic growth, $\Delta y_{t}$, is shown to positively and significantly influence electricity access in the total sample, with a coefficient of $0 . 00 4^{* * *}$, which implies that as economies expand, access to electricity generally improves. However, this relationship is not significant in developed countries, likely because these countries already have widespread electricity access. In developing countries, although the coefficient, 0.01 is positive, it lacks statistical significance, indicating that factors beyond economic growth, such as governance and infrastructure, could play more pivotal roles in expanding access to electricity.

The impact of coal and oil prices on electricity access is particularly telling. For the total sample, both coal and oil prices positively and significantly affect electricity access. This suggests that increases in energy prices may lead to higher revenues for energy producers, potentially allowing for greater investment in expanding electricity infrastructure, and thereby improving access ([Joskow, 2006](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b34)). In contrast, in developed countries, the relationship between coal prices and electricity access is small, negative, and statistically insignificant. This likely reflects a shift towards renewable energy sources in these regions, reducing the dependency on fossil fuel prices. For developing countries coal, and oil are positive, the coefficients are not statistically significant, indicating that price changes alone may not directly translate into improved electricity access due to other overriding challenges, such as limited infrastructure and investment ([Fattouh and El-Katiri, 2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b23)).

Temperature change, $\Delta T e m p_{t}$ is shown to have a negative and significant impact on electricity access in the total sample. This finding suggests that rising temperatures could strain electricity systems, especially in regions where infrastructure is not robust enough to handle increased demand or climate impacts. However, this effect is not significant in developed countries, while in developing countries, the relationship is positive, although not significant, possibly indicating the varying impacts of temperature changes on electricity access depending on the infrastructure and climate resilience of the region.

Political stability, measured by changes in the Polity2 index, significantly and negatively affects electricity access in the whole sample and in both developed and developing countries. This indicates that political instability generally hinders efforts to expand electricity access, likely due to disruptions in governance, investment, regulations, and infrastructure development. The positive and statistically significant coefficient, $0 . 00 7^{* * *}$, for the squared term, $\Delta P o l i t y 2 S q_{t}$ in the whole sample suggests a nonlinear relationship in which both very high and very low levels of political stability could have complex effects on electricity access, possibly due to changes in policy or governance structures during such transitions.

Geopolitical risks, $\Delta G p r c h_{t}$ show a nuanced impact on electricity access. For the total sample, higher geopolitical risks are associated with a significant increase in electricity access, possibly reflecting increased efforts to secure and expand the electricity infrastructure in response to instability. However, in developed countries, the impact of geopolitical risks is negative and insignificant, suggesting that such risks may not be a significant factor to disrupt already stable electricity systems. In the context of developing countries, the absence of significant coefficents suggests that geopolitical factors might not be the primary factor in affecting electricity access. However, the presence of positive and weakly significant coefficient of the squared terms of Gprch suggests that policymakers in those countries might consider geopolitical factors in planning so that electricity access can be increased to promote energy security. However, the results should be interpreted with caution as specification focused on developing countries suffers from weak autocorrelation. In order to avoid such an econometric issue, we use the share of news articles on geopolitical risks before indexing as a proxy of geopolitical risks. The new findings are reported in [Table 16](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl16). As in [Table 15](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl15), [Table 16](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl16), we see a non-linear impacts of geopolitical risks on electricity access suggesting that initially countries increasingly electrify the economy for energy security during the crisis. However, electrification decreases in medium term, perhaps the return of dominant and cheap fossil fuel energy demotivates governments in pursuing expensive projects. Most importantly, the use of new proxy of geopolitical risks avoids the presence of weakly autocorrelation issue observed previously while preserving major findings.

## 4. Geopolitical risks and supply side impacts

As of now, we focused on quantifying the impacts of geopolitical risks on energy demand. Although energy demand is important to understand the dynamics of geopolitical risks in the energy market, the supply side might be equally vulnerable to external shocks, such as conflicts, political instability, and sanctions. Historical events like 1973 oil embargo and the recent Ukraine–Russia war have shown that geopolitical tensions impact energy supplies, increasing energy price volatility and hence impacting energy demand. To better understand the effects of geopolitical risks on the energy market, it is logical to assess the relationship between geopolitical risks and energy variables on the supply side. In this subsection, we attempt to do so. For that purpose, we estimate the impacts of geopolitical risks on coal and oil production. As in the demand side, the main explanatory variable is geopolitical risks. Other dependent variables include production capacity, regulatory quality, oil price, coal prices, and investment in the energy sector. In addition, we also interact geopolitical risks with plant production capacity to assess whether strong domestic production capacity can help mitigate the negative impacts of geopolitical risks on fuel production. [Table 14](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl14) reports the results.

The Table shows a difference between the responses in coal and oil markets. From the table, we can see that coal production is influenced by historical price trends, coal price, and political and institutional shocks. From the table, we can see that previous coal production is positively and significantly related to the present coal production. This suggests an inertia in coal production trends. This finding is in line with the literature such as [Hamilton (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b28) who argue that energy production shows an inertia often due to infrastructure lock-ins and gradual resource depletion. Additionally, we can see that coal price returns have significant and positive impacts on coal production, signaling that producers respond to price incentives by increasing production, a phenomenon documented by [Kilian (2009)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b36). The statistically significant coefficients on the plant capacity for coal suggest that the continued improvement in energy infrastructure increases the energy production of this particular source of energy. This finding is consistent with [Joskow (2011)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b35). The most important explanatory variable, geopolitical risks, shows positive and significant impacts on coal production. For example, geopolitical risks significantly increase coal production, which might be because coal producer countries ramp up coal production in expectation of geopolitical risks which negatively affects oil production. This result is in line with the idea of supply manipulation amid uncertainty as explained in [Yergin (2006)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b65).

In addition to geopolitical risks, coal production is positively affected by investment, suggesting financing remains an important factor of output expansion. Lastly, the regulatory quality shows significant and negative coefficients on coal production, suggesting that a stricter environmental standard designed for cleaner environment limits coal production.

In contrast, oil production seems to be insignificantly impacted by historical oil production, market prices, and political and institutional situations. This could be due to the presence of institutional mechanisms such as OPEC production quota system that limits individual countries’ ability to ramp up oil production in face of geopolitical risks. As noted by [Pierru et al. (2020)](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#b45), OPEC coordination mechanism might bring production stability in the time of heightened geopolitical risks. Overall, [Table 17](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl17) suggests the coal production is more responsive to geopolitical risks than oil production, highlighting a strong and resilient nature of oil production market.

## 5. Conclusions and discussions

This paper provides a comprehensive analysis of the complex relationship between geopolitical risks and energy markets, focusing on aspects such as energy consumption, trade, and the transition to renewable energy. Our findings add to the growing body of literature that seeks to understand how geopolitical uncertainty influences energy dynamics, especially in light of recent global developments.

The analysis reveals that while geopolitical risks do not directly and significantly impact total energy consumption, the relationship becomes more nuanced when specific energy sources and regional differences are considered. In high-income countries, geopolitical risks tend to drive an increase in energy consumption, probably due to the need to secure energy supplies during uncertain times. Conversely, in developing countries, heightened geopolitical risks not only increase the consumption level but also the intensity, suggesting vulnerabilities in energy systems caused by higher level of political instability, military operations, or inefficiencies in energy supply chain.

The study also finds that geopolitical risks have a differential impact on the use of fossil fuel and renewable energy. In developed countries, these risks negatively affect the growth of renewable energy consumption, highlighting the vulnerability of renewable energy investments to global uncertainties. In contrast, in developing countries, geopolitical risks appear to lead to an increased reliance on fossil fuels, reflecting a continued dependency on traditional energy sources during periods of instability.

Additionally, geopolitical risks influence international energy trade, with a positive impact on fuel exports and imports beyond a certain thresholds. This suggests that energy-exporting countries can boost exports to capitalize on higher market prices during periods of heightened geopolitical risk, while energy-importing countries might also need to keep on relying on imported fuel to maintain their energy security.

Moreover, the study highlights a change in the impact of geopolitical risks on energy consumption over time. During the Cold War era, geopolitical risks had a substantial influence on energy supply and consumption, largely driven by superpower rivalries and regional conflicts. However, in the modern era, the direct impact of geopolitical risks on energy consumption has diminished, probably due to diversification of energy sources, technological advancements, and the emergence of new energy players.

These findings underscore the complex interplay between geopolitical risks and energy markets, suggesting that policymakers and investors must consider these nuances when making decisions. The results particularly emphasize the importance of energy diversification and the transition to renewable energy as strategies to mitigate the risks associated with geopolitical instability. The study also points to the need for more research on the long-term implications of geopolitical risks on the energy sector, especially in the context of global efforts to transition to a low-carbon economy. Understanding how geopolitical uncertainties influence investment decisions in the energy sector, particularly in developing countries, will be crucial to ensure a stable and sustainable energy future. Overall, this paper contributes to the literature by offering new insights into the effects of geopolitical risks on energy markets and provides a foundation for future research on the intersection of geopolitics and energy policy (see [Table 18](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl18), [Table 19](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325006413#tbl19)).

## Cited by (3)

*   ### [Global oil trading networks: Structural patterns and geopolitical risks](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0140988325007923)

2025, Energy Economics  Show abstract The intensification of global oil market linkages has elevated the relevance of geopolitical risk (GPR) as a determinant of trade patterns and energy security exposure. This study investigates how international oil trade has been reshaped over the period 2009–2023, with a particular emphasis on the role of GPR in influencing network centrality across the global trading system. Using monthly trade data from 42 economies, we combine network topology methods with panel regression analysis to map the evolution of oil trade interdependencies. Results indicate that the United States, Russia, Germany, China, and India function as core hubs that anchor and expand the architecture of global oil flows. Regression results reveal that heightened GPR systematically increases network centrality and trade intensity. Mechanism analysis points to two reinforcing channels: geopolitical disruptions inhibit the diffusion of renewable energy and simultaneously magnify characteristics associated with the resource curse, thereby strengthening reliance on fossil fuels. In addition, spatial spillovers are evident, as regional shocks propagate outward and alter trade structures well beyond the originating countries. These findings underscore the necessity of embedding geopolitical dimensions into domestic and international energy policy design, as oil trade networks are susceptible to risk transmission across borders.    
*   ### [Dynamic and Balanced Monitoring of the Path to Carbon Neutrality Among European Union Countries: The DETA Framework for Energy Transition Assessment](https://doi.org/10.3390/en19020358)

2026, Energies     
*   ### [Electricity Price Volatility and the Performance of Machine Learning Forecasting Models in European Energy Markets](https://doi.org/10.3390/en18246535)

2025, Energies

# What leaders are saying about the renewed geopolitics of energy

Apr 2, 2026

![Image 69: Birds fly near the Jag Vasant vessel transferring LPG at a port after transiting the Strait of Hormuz amid energy supply disruptions linked to the U.S-Israeli conflict with Iran, in Mumbai, India, April 1, 2026. ](https://assets.weforum.org/article/image/large_-5EEanHFCzYX6wpH47l6aPUuYEip0cJXXk6I6-P7irA.JPG)

In March, the Forum convened its Chief Geopolitical Executives Community, where leaders exchanged on the geopolitics of energy.

Image: REUTERS/Francis Mascarenhas

#### Geopolitics

Follow

This article is part of:[Centre for Regions, Trade and Geopolitics](https://www.weforum.org/centres/centre-for-regions-trade-and-geopolitics/)

*   The Middle East conflict is upturning energy markets locally and globally, and exposing a new method of geopolitical confrontation.
*   The conflict is also extending beyond oil, disrupting diplomacy globally between some of the world's most important economies.
*   The World Economic Forum convened its [Chief Geopolitical Executives Community](https://initiatives.weforum.org/geopolitics-insight-series/home), where leaders shared insights on the new geopolitics of energy.

Energy and geopolitics have once again become deeply intertwined. For much of the post-Cold War period, relative energy stability was often taken for granted, even as markets remained globally interconnected. That assumption is now breaking down.

From supply shocks to transit reconfigurations, energy is re-emerging as a central component of national security considerations and global power dynamics.

Against this backdrop, the World Economic Forum convened its Chief Geopolitical Executives Community on 10 March, for a discussion in partnership with the Atlantic Council on “Power Plays: The New Geopolitics of Energy”.

Bringing together senior executives focused on geopolitics, trade and global affairs, the session explored recent developments in the Middle East and their implications for energy markets and systems, as well as how wider geopolitical shifts are reshaping energy strategies and, in turn, how energy considerations are influencing geopolitics.

Here is what the world’s top political and energy executives had to say about the new geopolitics of energy.

## Uncertainty and politicization of energy lies ahead

The war in Iran has brought a critical share of global energy flows under acute strain. The Strait of Hormuz, which typically carries [around 20–25% of global seaborne oil trade and about 20% of global liquefied natural gas trade](https://www.iea.org/about/oil-security-and-emergency-response/strait-of-hormuz), has seen flows fall to a fraction of normal levels. Some Gulf producers have taken production offline or curtailed operations as export routes remain constrained.

Against this backdrop, the International Energy Agency has described the crisis as [“the greatest global energy security threat in history](https://www.ft.com/content/09524a74-db3c-4aef-b4f7-51eda3068320?syn-25a6b1a6=1)”, with volumes of oil and gas affected surpassing those seen during the 1970s oil shocks. While it remains too early to determine the full duration and long-term implications of the conflict, early indications suggest that restoring energy flows from the Gulf could take months, even in the event of de-escalation.

In the highly integrated system that long defined the global economy, weaponizing energy carried high risks due to deep interdependencies and strong economic ties. While today’s geopolitical landscape is more fragmented, the global energy system itself remains deeply interconnected, meaning that disruptions in one region can rapidly reverberate across markets worldwide. This combination of geopolitical fragmentation and market integration is reinforcing the role of energy, alongside other forms of critical infrastructure, as a powerful instrument of statecraft, while amplifying the global impact of its disruption, as reflected in emergency measures taken by several energy-importing countries.

Developments in Iran illustrate this shift starkly. Energy transit routes are themselves becoming operational levers in geopolitical confrontation, where the disruption or threat of disruption can generate immediate global effects.

The [coordinated release of 400 million barrels from International Energy Agency emergency reserves](https://www.iea.org/news/iea-member-countries-to-carry-out-largest-ever-oil-stock-release-amid-market-disruptions-from-middle-east-conflict), the largest such intervention in its history, underscored both the scale of the shock and the limits of available policy responses. At the same time, oil prices have surged and remained highly volatile, fluctuating above $100 per barrel amid continued uncertainty over supply. Together, these dynamics illustrate the immediate market disruption and broader strategic consequences associated with the weaponization of energy in the current geopolitical environment.

## What happens in the Middle East has global implications

Developments in Iran underscore a broader reality: despite a more fragmented and contested global landscape, markets and geopolitics are highly integrated.

Since the outbreak of the conflict in the Middle East and the subsequent disruption to oil flows through the Strait of Hormuz, prospects for renewed diplomatic pathways in Ukraine have become more uncertain. The Gulf conflict is also beginning to affect broader diplomatic engagement, including the postponement of a planned summit between US President Donald Trump and Chinese President Xi Jinping, underscoring how rapidly evolving developments in the region are shaping global relations.

But the central global dynamic has seen disruption to energy markets. With significant volumes of Gulf oil unable to transit, the United States announced a temporary easing of certain oil-related sanctions to mitigate supply shortages and stabilize markets. Such measures may alter global supply dynamics, including by increasing the availability of Russian crude, providing a potential boon for the Russian economy. Shifts in energy flows and pricing could, in turn, affect the broader geopolitical calculus surrounding the Ukraine conflict.

## How major global players are responding

Likely US policy responses include greater emphasis on domestic production, reduced reliance on China-linked electricity and mineral supply chains and renewed interest in nuclear energy.

China has sought to position itself as a source of stability amid growing geopolitical volatility, using recent high-level engagements with global business leaders to emphasize the resilience of its economy and the reliability of its supply chains. This contrast in positioning reflects the broader strategic competition shaping global energy and economic systems.

Beyond bilateral dynamics, the scale of current disruptions is also driving renewed global coordination, as industry leaders, policymakers and investors gather at [major international energy forums](https://www.japantimes.co.jp/business/2026/03/23/markets/middle-east-houston-davos-energy/) to assess supply risks and market responses, but also to explore evolving geopolitical alignments and new forms of collaboration reshaping the global energy landscape.

# Top geopolitical risks 2025: Energy insights

Opportunities for businesses to navigate uncertainty with confidence

* * *

In a connected world, the energy, natural resources and chemicals sectors are impacted significantly by geopolitics. Respondents to the [KPMG 2024 Energy, Natural Resources and Chemicals CEO Outlook](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/value-creation/global-ceo-outlook-survey/energy-natural-resources-chemicals.html) rank geopolitical complexities (55 percent) as the top challenge facing their companies, followed by economic uncertainty (43 percent)[1](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes).

As part of the [Top geopolitical risks 2025](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast-2025.html) paper from KPMG International, this report considers the risks – as well as the opportunities – from tariffs, competition for resources, a heightened regulatory environment, supply chain disruption, climate change, and other phenomena. In the midst of geoeconomic confrontation and a decline in the effectiveness of international rulemaking, corporate leaders need to monitor geopolitical shifts and continue to adapt to constant change.

KPMG’s midyear check-in comes at a pivotal moment. Heading into the second half of 2025, the economic and geopolitical signals are intensifying. From global competition and evolving transatlantic trade talks to shifting conflicts and tariff regimes, companies are navigating a complex web of global uncertainty that shows no signs of dissipating.

KPMG’s recent global paper [Energy transition investment outlook: 2025 and beyond](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/esg/energy-transition-investment-outlook-2025-and-beyond.html) surveys 1,400 senior executives from companies actively investing in the energy transition. More than two-thirds (72 percent) of respondents say that, in spite of high interest rates and geopolitical volatility, investment in energy transition assets is increasing rapidly. However, 75 percent continue to invest in fossil fuel energy – indicating that a mix of traditional and renewable energy may persist for some time.[2](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) As the [2025 Statistical Review of World Energy](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/esg/statistical-review-of-world-energy.html) (produced in collaboration with KPMG) reveals, the demands of an energy-hungry world are driving growth in both renewables and oil, gas and coal, as part of a disorderly energy transition.[3](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

The chemicals sector is experiencing a long down-cycle, and our conversations with senior execs in this industry suggest no immediate end in sight. Indeed, the specter of high inflation and interest rates and low growth threaten profitability across capital expenditure-intensive industries like energy, natural resources and chemicals.

The report is intended for independent boards of directors and senior business executives, particularly Chief Executive Officers, Chief Risk Officers and other key decision-makers responsible for strategic planning and risk management within energy, natural resources and chemicals companies.

* * *

## Five key geopolitical risks and trends facing energy, natural resources, and chemicals companies

1.    Tectonic shifts in power 
2.    Fragmented regulatory and tax 
3.    Technology landscape 
4.    Multiple threats to infrastructure 
5.    Pressures on workforces 

### Tectonic shifts in power, economic centers and trade

As a primary input to every aspect of human life, energy is at the center of geopolitical change, with the growing demand for electricity to power data centers driving renewable energy production. Despite its abundance of fossil fuels, the US needs all energy sources to power its drive towards electrification for electric vehicle (EVs), data centers and cooling. China currently leads the manufacturing of solar panels, wind turbines and batteries, and has tied up much of the access to the rare earth minerals that underpin renewable energy sources. China is also soaring ahead in its bid to become energy-independent, adding double the amount of renewable energy of the US, Europe, and India combined in 2024. Europe, on the other hand is energy poor and requires alliances to secure supplies. Additionally, renewables momentum in Europe has stalled somewhat, with growth of just 7 percent in 2024, due to high financing costs, supply chain bottlenecks and permitting obstacles.[4](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

Although clean energy has huge potential, it is subject to intense competition, putting a downward pressure on returns – including in China, where the government has put floors on renewable energy and EV prices to prevent extreme competition.The renewables sector is in danger of suffering “involution” – a race to the bottom, characterized by sales growth yet declining margins.

Tariffs are forcing a radical rethink of where companies source both their energy and the materials that help produce energy. Increased transnational professional mobility challenges may also exacerbate skills shortages and labor cost predictability.

In the face of tariffs, supply chain disruption, and geopolitical uncertainty, business and investor confidence has fallen, which has weakened demand and lowered prices for crude oil, petrochemicals, and coal. A decline in global trade, as well as a recession, could even reduce demand for energy – especially oil and gas – directly impacting the sector’s revenue. On the other hand, with countries like the US aiming to produce more fossil fuels, production could potentially rise, although excess production could cause costs to fall, which again hits margins. Peak oil may be a few years away, but we may already have reached peak oil prices.

Geopolitical leaders are increasingly signaling long-term decoupling in critical sectors. “The trajectory of international relations will likely impact the business environment significantly,” noted Stefano Moritsch, KPMG Global Geopolitical Lead. He pointed to ongoing tensions, transatlantic negotiations, and a shifting trade architecture as key developments for the remainder of 2025. The US has threatened to impose punitive tariffs on importers of Russian oil, should Russia fail to agree a peace deal with Ukraine. Such moves signal confidence that swings production in other regions (including the US) can fill the gap to satisfy demand.[5](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) In South America, the Mercosur trading bloc between Brazil, Paraguay, Uruguay and Argentina could be under threat if the latter were to leave to secure a trade agreement with the US. Argentina's departure could reduce regional energy trade in natural gas and electricity, slowing down the renewable energy transition.

Centers of oil and gas generation, namely the Middle East, are striving to reduce their dependence on energy for national revenue, and are encouraging foreign business, commerce and leisure investment. Nevertheless, these countries currently rely heavily on oil income, and, after a couple of years of production quotas, the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting Countries (OPEC) has signaled its intent to release barrels to the market,[6](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) which would push down prices further.

### Opportunities for energy, natural resources and chemicals companies

The mining sector is attracting demand from international investors for materials – like nickel – crucial to batteries and other renewable energy components. This spells positive news for businesses in mineral-rich regions. Latin America, especially Brazil, is pushing ahead fast in renewables, presenting opportunities for investment in this sector. Globally, the renewables market should continue to flourish to keep up with the cloud and AI revolution, and give countries greater energy security. Nuclear power may also enjoy a boom, to reduce reliance on energy imports. For the first time in several years, nuclear power rose as a proportion of total global energy generation in 2024, to 5 percent, mainly due to increased output in France and Japan.[7](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)The Eurasia Group’s Top Risks 2025 argues that even if the move to renewables stalls, “…the global energy transition — which includes both renewables and nuclear — will power forward.”[8](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

Any rise in fossil fuel production creates demand for major projects, especially refineries in countries like the US, which has limited capacity. As the world becomes less globalized, energy, natural resources and chemicals companies may need to pivot to new regions, ‘friendshoring’ and localizing their supply chains, and adapting to new regional alliances.

### A complex, fragmented regulatory and tax environment

Regulations and tax are evolving at different speeds in different geographies. The U.S., for example, is rolling back many sustainability regulations. Minimum global tax is becoming adopted by many countries, while others are withdrawing from multilateral tax policy.

A fragmentation of tax regimes, along with sudden changes, creates greater uncertainty over return on investment (ROI) in different markets – and could slow corporate investment cycles. It can be a challenge for international energy, natural resources and chemicals companies to keep up with the fast pace of change. According to the KPMG 2024 Energy, Natural Resources and Chemicals CEO Outlook, trade regulation is the number one factor (73 percent) likely to impact organizational prosperity over the next three years.[9](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

In the US, for instance, businesses that have made investments on the back of Inflation Reduction Act (IRA) incentives are likely to be impacted by the ‘One Big Beautiful Bill Act,’ which is hastening the end of tax breaks for solar and wind projects, as well as for EVs and residential energy efficiency.[10](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) This creates uncertainty, not least because of the sizeable foreign investment in the renewables sector. In many other parts of the world, however, the push for decarbonization is expected to persist, with favorable government conditions.

Even though China is yet to implement global minimum tax, Chinese energy companies involved in making solar panels, wind turbines and batteries in other countries could still receive higher tax bills, which could cause a rethink of where they invest.

Strict environmental standards in some geographies restrict extraction and production of critical minerals, which could leave companies trailing competitors in countries with more relaxed regulations. In regions like Europe, the innovative potential of artificial intelligence (AI) could also be held back by strict regulations.

Diane Swonk, Chief Economist at KPMG US, emphasized the indirect costs of policy uncertainty, warning: “Uncertainty is its own tax on the economy. Businesses must track macroeconomic indicators closely, particularly in this regulatory flux.”

### Opportunities for energy, natural resources and chemicals companies

A rolling back of regulations in the US could lessen bottlenecks such as permitting and accelerate the progress of major energy projects to meet the data center thirst for electricity. And US liquid natural gas (LNG) companies should continue to increase their exports to countries keen to reduce the trade gap with the US. In recent years, the US has stepped up to become the world’s largest LNG exporter, filling the gap left by Russia.[11](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

Global cooperation on climate change, including the Paris Agreement and Climate Promise 2025, can drive investment in renewable energy and promote sustainable development.

Energy, natural resources and chemicals companies should understand performance levers, to embed their capacity to adapt and respond to regulatory and tax changes and monitor developments. Companies should continually review their asset portfolios, to acquire or dispose of assets to adapt to evolving tax structures.

### A fast-moving and politicized technology landscape

Shifting alliances (based upon national security concerns) and fragmented regulations add complexity to technology investment decisions. Regulators are struggling to keep pace with new Generative AI (Gen AI, referred to simply as AI below) solutions. China’s dominance of critical minerals supply chains and processing gives it significant control over chips and their component materials.

The US seeks continued technology leadership, putting pressure to build out data centers at pace and develop domestic chip manufacturing capabilities to counter a possible shortage of essential technologies.

AI has the potential to be a game-changer in optimizing grid efficiency and distribution. AI/Gen AI can help radically improve and scale up renewable energy systems, identifying optimum locations for wind and solar facilities, monitoring performance, providing predictive maintenance, and enhancing grid efficiency.

However, national security concerns could slow down AI adoption, if it results in companies having a limited selection of suppliers. Geopolitical competition in AI and other technologies (like quantum computing) is creating technological blocs around the US and China, jeopardizing international cooperation and access. Data protection and privacy regulations are also obliging energy, natural resources and chemicals companies to use local cloud and data center providers, in certain instances.

Nancy Chase, Risk Management Leader, KPMG Canada, highlighted the role of AI in dynamic scenario planning. “Leveraging data to model and understand potential impacts is essential, as companies aim to maintain real-time visibility in a rapidly evolving tech environment.”

Data centers, and AI factories used to power AI, have a huge thirst for energy, which, is placing immense pressure on energy systems around the world. In the recent KPMG US paper _From crisis to opportunity_, 76 percent of respondents believe electricity usage in data centers will increase by more than 10 percent annually over the next decade. And 57 percent feel the current pace of energy development is too slow to meet increased demands for electricity from the growth in data centers.[12](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

At the same time, AI has potential to improve energy-efficiency, dramatically reducing the amount of energy required to cool data centers and other buildings. As energy security and independence become ever bigger government priorities, the energy demands of AI put the spotlight on energy security and self-sufficiency.

### Opportunities for energy, natural resources and chemicals companies

By creating modular, adaptable IT infrastructure, companies across the sector can build technology stacks that meet national regulatory standards, whilst maintaining a global design. This might include clustering platforms, data centers and other tech, and diversifying into cloud services distributed across multiple geographies, to avoid regulatory and security issues. By building alliances with critical suppliers, they can help ensure availability of key hardware, and scale up major energy projects, especially in renewables. Our KPMG Global tech report 2024 finds that 86 percent of organizations plan to invest in cloud ‘as-a-service’ (XaaS) over the next year.[13](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) Technology alliances are playing a critical role in scaling major energy projects, especially in renewables.

Energy, natural resources and chemicals companies can also build trust in AI by respecting local sensitivities. And, amid rising cybersecurity threats from malicious nation-state actors, investment in cybersecurity can strengthen critical infrastructure and enterprise systems.

### Multiple threats to supply chains, assets and infrastructure

Geopolitical rivalries, trade protectionism, conflict, competition for resources, cyberattacks, and climate events place severe strains on globally exposed energy, natural resources and chemicals companies. Wars and tensions pose rising threats to key shipping choke points. Countries are adopting protectionist measures to safeguard and diversify their energy and critical minerals supply chains. However, after almost two years of conflict in the Middle East, energy prices have remained largely stable, with only temporary fluctuations. Even in Europe, where the war in Ukraine has raged since 2022, prices stabilized once LNG supplies reoriented.

Energy infrastructure is under a constant and growing cybersecurity threat (often from nation states), heightened by the retreat from globalization, with the world evolving into geopolitical blocs of ‘friendly’ countries. The increasing number of climate events, such as floods, fires and hurricanes, are damaging assets, particularly power and utilities facilities. Not surprisingly, extreme weather events are considered one of the top risks in the World Economic Forum’s (WEF) Global Risks Report 2025. Looking 10 years ahead, the WEF’s top three risks are all environmental: extreme weather events; biodiversity loss and ecosystem collapse; and critical change to earth systems.[14](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes)

According to our KPMG International report [Turning the tide in scaling renewables](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/esg/energy-transition/turning-the-tide-in-scaling-renewables.html), 84 percent of respondents say geopolitical challenges are causing substantial delays in, or even the abandonment of renewable energy projects. Additionally, 61 percent of renewable energy industry stakeholders cite supply chain risks as a significant obstacle to scaling renewable energy projects.[15](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) As the main supplier for solar equipment, China holds many cards in the renewables race, and geopolitical tensions could disrupt exports and stall other nations’ green energy progress.

Dr. Brendan Rynne, Chief Economist for KPMG in Asia Pacific, warned of region-specific pressures from tariffs and Japanese bond yields. “The impacts on South Korea and Japan will be pronounced, signaling that shifts in trade dynamics may accelerate in late 2025.”

With limited finances, power and utilities companies have to build out infrastructure to satisfy growing demand, while also burying power lines to make grids more resilient. This puts pressure on investment priorities, while regulations can hinder their ability to raise prices to fund these investments. Energy companies may also suffer regulatory penalties for disruption to services or failure to prevent damage. Insurance is a further challenge, with companies struggling to cover their assets against extreme weather events.

One of the main bottlenecks to expanding mineral extraction is refining and processing, which takes time to develop. Countries with their own reserves want to move down the value chain from exploring to refining and production, and seek investments to enable this shift.

### Opportunities for energy, natural resources and chemicals companies

Creating circular supply chains and production can help reduce dependency on raw materials, by embedding re-use and recycling into the design of products and infrastructure – including waste energy recycling systems. And companies can diversify sources of important minerals to hedge against supply chain disruption. Governments in mineral-rich nations are also likely to encourage investment in downstream value chains, notably refining and processing.

Additionally, it’s vital to invest in protecting assets and infrastructure against wind, flooding, forest fires and drought, to strengthen existing infrastructure and, where possible, construct new facilities in safer locations. Companies can formalize their risk policies and monitoring, and use software to improve their climate modeling, assess potential climate scenarios, and quantify their exposure to these risks.

### Demographic, technological, and cultural pressures on workforces

The demographic timebomb of an aging population is impacting workforces in many geographies. With older workers retiring, a decline in apprenticeships in some markets, and younger workers less likely to join or stay in the sector, companies are finding it hard to access the skills they need. And, with automation replacing many manual tasks, the reliance on technical skills increases operational vulnerability. For example, drones and internet of things (IoT) devices and sensors have huge possibilities for surveyance, maintenance and construction, but require people with expertise to manage and oversee their use.

Respondents to the KPMG’s Global 2024 Energy, Natural Resources and Chemicals CEO Outlook say that a lack of technical capability and skills (43 percent) is the second biggest barrier to implementing Gen AI.[16](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/our-insights/risk-and-regulation/top-risks-forecast/energy.html#footnotes) Additionally, efforts to reduce immigration decreases the availability of migrant labor.

Yael Selfin, Chief Economist for KPMG UK and for the Europe, Middle East and Africa regions, emphasized the demographic and technological trends with greater certainty: “Rather than wait for clarity on trade policy, businesses should act now on high-certainty areas like public debt, aging populations, and technology evolution.”

### Opportunities for energy, natural resources and chemicals companies

There’s a growing interest in renewable energy as an exciting career, both in terms of cutting-edge technology and combating climate change, positioning the sector as a critical part of the solution to the energy transition. Companies in this sector can highlight the potential benefits to attract a new generation of talent.

Organizations should also seek to shape a dynamic and adaptable workforce through upskilling, virtual learning and organizational redesign. A balanced, hybrid work environment, with a rebooted employee value proposition, can help satisfy the expectations of younger workers.

* * *

## Geopolitical risk management as a way of life

Faced with continued volatility and uncertainty, energy, natural resources and chemicals companies can gain a competitive edge, by treating geopolitical risk as an asset as well as a threat. This means assessing both the opportunities and the potential negative impacts of the five key risks outlined in this paper:

1.   ###### Companies can address tectonic shifts in power, economic centers and trade through continued focus on the energy transition, and by seizing fresh opportunities in fossil fuel and minerals extraction and refining. 
2.   ###### In response to a complex, fragmented regulatory and tax environment, organizations can improve their monitoring capabilities, review asset portfolios and adapt to renewable energy tax incentives. 
3.   ###### Investment in cybersecurity, trusted use of AI, selective technology partnerships, and modular IT infrastructure can help companies adapt to a fast-moving and politicized technology landscape. 
4.   ###### Amidst multiple threats to supply chains, assets and infrastructure, energy, natural resources and chemicals companies should create circular business models and invest in making assets more resilient. 
5.   ###### Demographic, technological, and cultural pressures on workforces present opportunities to highlight the use of exciting new technologies in sectors like renewables. 

Amidst prevailing uncertainty, the one silver lining for energy and resources companies is continued energy demand growth. In such an environment, those businesses with disciplined capital allocation, robust planning and operations processes, and cost management capabilities, should be well-positioned to take advantage of opportunities.

#### We value your privacy

When you visit any website, it may store or retrieve information on your browser, mostly in the form of cookies. This information might be about you, your preferences or your device and is mostly used to make the site work as you expect it to. The information does not usually directly identify you, but it can give you a more personalized web experience. Because we respect your right to privacy, you can choose not to allow some types of cookies. Click on the different category headings to find out more and change our default settings. However, blocking some types of cookies may impact your experience of the site and the services we are able to offer. 

[Privacy statement](https://kpmg.com/xx/en/home/misc/privacy.html)

#### Strictly Necessary Cookies

Always Active

These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which essentially amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work.

Cookies Details‎

#### Functional Cookies

- [x] Functional Cookies 

These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.

Cookies Details‎

#### Performance Cookies

- [x] Performance Cookies 

These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.

Cookies Details‎

#### Targeting Cookies

- [x] Targeting Cookies 

These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners. They may be used by those companies through for instance collecting data from videos embedded in websites and aggregating it with other data in order display targeted advertising to web visitors across a broad range of their own and other websites. They do not store directly personal information, but are based on uniquely identifying your browser and internet device. If you do not allow these cookies, you will experience less targeted advertising.
